 
 
Weekly Snapshot - Jan 25, 2026
 
 
 Sony turns off the TV 
↳ Why it matters 
News broke this past week that Sony is ceding control of its home entertainment 
business to TCL, which will hold a majority stake in a newly formed company. 
Sony’s decision to spin off its television business, which among others 
manufactures Bravia products, marks more than a corporate restructuring. It 
reflects  a deeper shift in where value is created in the global tech industry. Control 
of a core hardware operation is moving from a Japanese icon to a Chinese 
manufacturer, reinforcing a long-term trend: electronics manufacturing is 
becoming increasingly commoditized, scale-driven and dominated by Chinese 
players. 
By the way, wherever you are in the world, expect many new Chinese brands you 
have never heard of to enter your market in the years to come. Times are changing, 
and so are TV shows and TV set manufacturers. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
This shift didn’t happen overnight. I remember participating in discussions more 
than a decade ago in Seoul, South Korea, where KIST and ETRI leaders and 
policymakers openly acknowledged their strength in devices and semiconductors, 
but their weakness in platforms and content, highlighting that Korea’s future 
strategy would focus precisely on closing that gap. Fast forward to today, and 
----------------Page (0) Break----------------
Korean movies win Oscars, K-pop dominates global charts, and Korean formats 
flood streaming platforms worldwide. 
Sony seems to be reading the same map—to be fair, they saw it before others and 
acquired Columbia Pictures back in 1989. Hardware builds reach, but content 
builds power. The strategic questions for any advanced or aspiring economy are no 
longer how to make better devices, but (1) how to control the core technologies 
behind them—semiconductors being the clearest example—and (2) how to design, 
own and scale cultural and digital assets globally. Sony’s decision feels less like 
retreat and more like focus. That said: can Japan rebuild its edge in 
semiconductors? How? 
 Space is getting crowded 
↳ Why it matters 
Earth’s orbit is becoming increasingly congested, and this past week global media 
reported that Chinese satellites and space debris forced SpaceX’s Starlink fleet to 
execute a record number of collision-avoidance manoeuvres in 2025. Looking 
ahead, things will only get worse — more crowded, more complicated, riskier and 
costlier. 
Thousands of satellites and millions of debris fragments are now moving at 
extreme speeds above us, dramatically increasing collision risks. A single impact 
can destroy assets worth hundreds of millions of dollars and generate even more 
debris, amplifying the problem. This congestion raises operational risk, insurance 
costs, and uncertainty for governments and private operators alike. 
At the same time, it creates a fast-growing market for space situational awareness: 
companies that can track objects, predict collisions, and provide timely alerts 
capable of saving satellites and entire constellations. A new ecosystem is emerging 
around this need, with players such as LeoLabs from the United States, Neuraspace 
----------------Page (1) Break----------------
from Portugal, Spaceflux    from the United Kingdom, Share My Space from France, 
and NorthStar Earth & Space from Canada positioning themselves as the future “air 
traffic  controllers” of Earth’s orbit. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
I often write about space in this Snapshot because it is rapidly becoming a major 
economy, I am deeply interested in new technologies and new industries, and I am 
part of Seldor Capital, a VC firm  that invests in space tech. And everything 
space-related is changing fast, attracting attention, new players and investment in 
new assets on Earth and far above our heads. As the space economy grows, new 
problems inevitably surface, opening space for new types of activities and players, 
as in any other economy that matures and becomes more sophisticated. Space 
traffic  management will soon resemble air traffic  control, but without a shared 
global authority, clear legal frameworks, or agreed-upon rules of responsibility. 
Those who can foresee now future necessities and act will be better positioned to 
profit in the future. 
To add complexity to the matter, in a fragmented geopolitical world, coordination 
becomes harder just as it becomes more necessary. Who is liable when objects 
collide? Who pays for cleanup? And who decides whose satellite moves first? 
These questions remain largely unanswered, yet they will define  space usability — 
and costs and risks — in the long run. 
 Africa’s got talent! Opportunities? 
↳ Why it matters 
Africa’s population is young and growing fast—the continent is projected to be 
home to 40% of the world’s population by the end of the century—and increasingly 
connected. It holds one of the largest untapped pools of human potential in the 
world. But unlocking that potential depends on providing quality education and 
skills development at scale and speed. Different pieces of news about education 
----------------Page (2) Break----------------
systems in Africa popped up last week. In South Africa, a pioneer private university 
was announced—until October 2025, private higher-education institutions could not 
be called “universities.” In Zimbabwe and Kenya, new initiatives to expand internet 
access in schools were launched. All of this is positive, but economic success and 
social stability are far from guaranteed, as education alone is not enough. 
Skills must evolve alongside economies, or societies risk producing trained people 
with no jobs to absorb them locally. Until now, well-trained people who could not 
find  jobs in local markets often looked for opportunities abroad–this is the classical 
brain drain problem–but things are changing. In today’s tense and troubled world, 
doors in advanced economies are closing to migrants and, more importantly, as AI 
and robotics advance, hundreds of millions of jobs will disappear globally, and 
those well-trained people and shopfloor   workers from emerging nations may no 
longer be needed in advanced economies or even in their local markets. A new 
dynamic is emerging, and past development paths are closing. 
Throughout history, to varying degrees, investments in education have paid 
off—and I firmly believe they will continue to—but the scenario is dramatically 
changing in the AI age, the window of opportunity for economies to catch up is 
narrowing and there is an absolute need for novel strategies. Countries coming 
from behind need to simultaneously accelerate talent development, skilling and 
upskilling to unprecedented speed and levels and with a focus on new skills—this 
requires massive resources—and bet heavily on entrepreneurship. The challenge is 
tremendous. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
When I visited Kenya not long ago, what struck me most was the sheer number of 
young people in public spaces on weekends: filming TikTok dances, creating 
content, rollerskating, hanging out with friends, just being there. There was energy 
and creativity, but also emptiness and a waste of human potential: 67% of Kenyans 
----------------Page (3) Break----------------
aged 15–34 are unemployed, many of them with a college education. Opportunities 
were not there, and I heard young Kenyans voicing their discontent with that reality. 
Young people in emerging and least developed economies are between a rock and a 
hard place. There are three overlapping realities: the skills young people acquire 
through education are not needed in the economy, either because they are below 
the level the market requires or because the market itself remains underdeveloped 
and cannot absorb those coming out of educational institutions; graduates cannot 
access global job markets due to immigration and labor regulations or the lack of 
skills required in more advanced economies; and jobs are disappearing globally. 
In the past, economies and societies had more time to adjust. That is no longer the 
case. Initiatives like the ones mentioned here need to scale 100×, maybe 1,000×, 
and they need completely updated content. A major shift in curricula, instructional 
approaches, education technology and scale is a must, not just in Africa. So the real 
question is: how can nations pull this off? Ideas? Examples?

Weekly Snapshot - Jan 18, 2026 
 
 Global politics has a new playbook 
↳ Why it matters 
Global alliances are being reshuffled   at a speed we have never seen before. What 
used to sound like fringe speculation such as the potential unravelling of the 
European Union is now openly debated in mainstream political and media circles. 
And this matters because the EU has been one of the most successful peace and 
integration projects in modern history, inspiring similar regional blocs from Asia to 
Mercosur. 
----------------Page (5) Break----------------
At the same time, geopolitics is no longer played slowly, behind closed doors, or 
strictly through institutions. It looks more like poker and chess being played 
simultaneously and in real time. The Canadian Prime Minister travels to Beijing while 
slashing tariffs on Chinese EVs, signaling a recalibration of ties with China. Shortly 
after, during a stop in Doha, he receives a personal invitation from President Trump 
to join discussions around Gaza. To close the day on Saturday, the U.S. president 
announced new tariffs on European allies “until they come to the negotiating table to 
sell Greenland.” 
The common thread is clear. Traditional diplomatic playbooks, protocols, and 
alliances are being bypassed, bent, disrupted and rewritten, all at the same time. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
What we are witnessing is not just a geopolitical transition, but a structural 
breakdown. International politics today is increasingly person to person, leader to 
leader, rather than institution to institution. Engagement has overtaken frameworks. 
Speed has overtaken process. 
The consequence is unsettling. News we read today may become irrelevant 
tomorrow, reframed the day after, or dismissed as misinformation soon after that. 
The system is moving faster than our ability to interpret it. And more importantly, we 
do not yet have the institutions, governance models, or leadership training systems 
to operate in this environment. 
This feels like the early days of a new type of statecraft, or perhaps a refreshed form 
of real-power statecraft. One where unpredictability is the norm and adaptability is 
the core skill. We are only at the beginning, and the real challenge will be preparing a 
new generation of leaders who can function in a world where structures lag reality. 
When global politics has a new playbook, everybody needs to get ready for a new 
game. 
----------------Page (6) Break----------------

 Markets alone will not solve for mosquitoes 
↳ Why it matters 
Every year, mosquito-borne diseases like dengue, zika and chikungunya cost the 
world about $100 billion. The burden falls disproportionately on emerging 
economies in the Southern hemisphere, where tropical climates prevail, economies 
are less developed than in the richer North, health systems are weaker and the social 
impact is far more dramatic. It is in this context that the recent dramatic reduction of 
dengue cases in Brazil, by more than 75%, is particularly relevant. 
Reducing the burden of dengue—and other diseases—is not just a health win. It frees 
up public resources, strengthens economies, improves quality of life and can create 
new avenues for growth. There is a second layer that matters just as much. At a time 
when science is increasingly politicized and basic public health principles are openly 
questioned, this case sends a powerful signal: science works and can deliver 
real-world impact at scale. Finally, this reframes public health as a fundamental 
investment. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
Among other things, Brazil is manufacturing and releasing mosquitoes infected with 
Wolbachia bacteria to curb disease transmission. What started as a local scientific   
effort is now expanding and inspiring similar programs worldwide through the World 
Mosquito Program. The scientist behind the initiative was named by Nature as one 
----------------Page (7) Break----------------
of the 10 most influential in the world in 2025. This is impact-oriented innovation 
applied to a complex societal problem. 
The lesson here is bigger than mosquitoes. Talent exists everywhere. What often 
lacks in emerging nations are stable institutional frameworks and economic 
environments, long-term funding, entrepreneurial pathways and the ambition to scale 
solutions beyond pilots. Yes, resources matter. But ambition, governance and 
institutional trust matter just as much. And yes, governments create markets and 
can help accelerate innovation too. 
Markets alone will not solve the mosquito problem. If you think they will, answer this: 
who, if not the public sector, would pay for the manufacturing and release of 
mosquitoes? It is a systemic challenge that requires public intervention. Ignore this 
basic reality and the bill comes later, with interest, for all of us to pay. According to a 
Brazilian government agency, the expectation is that for every $1 invested in the 
Wolbachia program, government savings in medicines, hospitalizations and 
treatments overall will range between $43.45 and $549.13. Not bad, right? 
↳ Read more here 
● https://tinyurl.com/4zcms53p 
● https://tinyurl.com/mt98bhd6 
● https://tinyurl.com/bddpkk6j 
● https://tinyurl.com/nhec2bw7 
 REMs mining goes ever deeper, on all fronts 
↳ Why it matters 
This past week, Japan launched the world’s first deep-sea trial to lift rare earth mud 
from 6 km below the seabed. Rare earth minerals (REMs) sit quietly at the core of 
modern life, and the Japanese economy depends on them. Electronics, digital 
----------------Page (8) Break----------------
infrastructure, automation, clean energy and defense systems — almost everything 
that defines  today’s economy relies on them. You would not be reading this on an 
electronic device without REMs, period. 
The REMs market is surprisingly small. For instance, Chinese REM exports to the 
United States amounted to about $170 million in 2024. Given the size of the U.S. 
economy, this is pocket change. But the importance of REMs is not about their price 
or the size of the market. It is about everything that would not exist without them. A 
disruption in REM supply to advanced economies like Japan or the U.S. would not 
cause losses in the tens or hundreds of millions, but would instead wipe out trillions 
of dollars in economic value — think of all the chips, computers, satellites, rockets 
and smartphones that would not be manufactured. 
This is not a problem that free markets can solve on their own. It is a strategic 
challenge that requires coordination, and that is where governments must step in. 
Small markets do not naturally attract the long-term investments needed to secure 
critical supply chains. This is precisely why the U.S., assuming markets alone would 
handle the issue, became so dependent on China. The contrast is stark. China 
engineered its dominance by aligning strategic foresight, state power, industrial 
policy and long-term planning. Much of the West left the future to market forces. 
Japan’s move to become the first country to mine rare earths from the deep sea is 
therefore not just about resources. It is also about governance models. About which 
systems can create durable competitive advantages in a world where speed and 
coordination are essential. And yes, we are talking about a government-backed 
project. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
Japan is an island nation, geographically close to China, an advanced economy 
deeply embedded in global supply chains and exposed in a fragmented world. 
Deep-sea mining is a bold attempt to reclaim strategic autonomy. But it comes with 
----------------Page (9) Break----------------
uncomfortable questions. We are pushing into fragile, poorly understood 
ecosystems. The environmental consequences of deep-sea extraction remain largely 
unknown. On a weakened planet, we are once again expanding into new frontiers 
without fully understanding the cost. This echoes earlier chapters of industrial 
progress, from deforestation to biodiversity loss, now extended beneath the ocean 
surface. 
There are alternatives in the REMs play. Such minerals exist in Africa, Australia and 
Latin America. But mining alone is not enough. Refining  is where real power sits, and 
this is where China still holds a decisive advantage. Building refining capacity takes 
time, technology and sustained political will. I have heard policymakers openly 
question whether it would take years or even a decade to catch up. This is the real 
dilemma. Do countries try to negotiate their way out of dependency? Or do they 
invest, patiently and expensively, in building new technological capabilities? Only a 
handful of nations can afford to answer that question in a way that truly shifts the 
balance. 
Japan is choosing action. I admire that, as I have always admired Japanese society 
for its astonishing achievements, capabilities, resilience, perfectionist drive and 
politeness. But I also hope this time is different in relation to the country’s 
relationship with the environment — think of whales, for instance. Different from the 
quiet destruction that often accompanies strategic ambition across the world. The 
deep sea is not an empty space, it is full of life. There are so many things we don't 
know, and the choices being made will echo far beyond minerals. 
↳ Read more here 
● https://tinyurl.com/4mhktxpr 
● https://tinyurl.com/4au54srk 
● https://tinyurl.com/nhjspktb

Weekly Snapshot - Jan 11, 2026 
 
The world has changed! 
It's 2026 and the world has changed. Have you noticed? 
The post–WWII order is crumbling (wasn’t it already, and we are now just seeing it?). 
Technology continues to accelerate and capital to concentrate. Social upheaval and 
youth protests are spreading globally. Truth has eroded in the age of AI. Uncertainty 
is building. Rules are being rewritten in real time. New and unusual alliances are 
forming. And only a few in the world can read the signals. 
Wow. That is a lot, isn’t it? Feeling overwhelmed? Get ready for what comes next. 
2026 has the potential to be one of those years remembered by history. It feels like 
1848, 1938, 1968... doesn’t it? Or... horse meets tiger and monkey squared. Things 
will never be the same again. 
Welcome to a brave new world. 
----------------Page (11) Break----------------

 Regimes are trembling 
↳ Why it matters 
From Caracas to Tehran to Havana, tension is in the air. We have all seen in the news 
the abduction of Nicolás Maduro in Venezuela. Cuba has been suffering for decades 
and will suffer even more in the aftermath of the strikes in Venezuela. We are now 
seeing in real time, as I write: across Iran, people are on the streets protesting, 
fighting, dying. The regimes in these three countries are trembling. 
What these cases have in common is striking: they are increasingly alone. No one is 
coming to rescue them. In this new world order, self-reliance is essential. And these 
are three failing economies, failing their populations. Pressure comes from within 
and, there's no secret, from the outside — from sanctions imposed by the U.S. and 
Europe to military threats and interventions. But there is another layer to this story 
and, in the end, though, it all comes back to failing institutional and economic 
models, as the participation of bazaar merchants in the protests in Iran suggest. 
Beyond the countries in question, the realization that self-reliance matters is likely to 
drive a new wave of defense investment across the world. In most cases, this will be 
a simple waste of money, as asymmetries are gigantic and only a handful of 
countries can realistically think about confronting the big powers. 
----------------Page (12) Break----------------
↳ My thoughts on it 
In one of my previous lives, I had the chance to visit Havana, Caracas and Tehran. My 
first impression in Havana was simple and powerful: this place lacks color. Caracas, 
where I have been many times, always felt tough — a city marked by violence in daily 
interactions, where trust sits at the very bottom of society. Tehran was different. My 
visit was short, but walking through commercial areas and bazaars, it felt more open 
than I expected. At the same time, I constantly had the sensation (sensation?) of 
being watched. 
In all three places, I had the same underlying impression: they could be very different. 
They could be much better. I have met and world with many people from these 
countries. I have Iranian-American friends who are incredibly smart, entrepreneurial 
and creative. What Persian civilization contributed over millennia — in science, 
technology, arts, literature and poetry — is extraordinary. Creativity and human 
potential are there. 
But the world has changed. In the new global order taking shape, it will be 
increasingly difficult  for countries to confront major regional powers if they lack 
economic strength and internal resilience. When basic economic principles are 
broken, everything else becomes a non-starter, and economies directly controlled by 
the military are doomed to fail. Make your bets and get ready for the shake-up.

 Listening is learning (not just for humans) 
----------------Page (13) Break----------------
↳ Why it matters 
A groundbreaking study published in Science this past week reveals that a rare group 
of dogs, dubbed Gifted Word Learners (GWLs) — think border collies, shepherds, 
labradors, mini schnauzers — can acquire names for objects simply by overhearing 
human conversations, without being directly addressed. Much like 18- to 
23-month-old human infants, these dogs successfully learned new object labels both 
when owners spoke to them and when they merely eavesdropped on conversations 
about the objects. In controlled tests, each label was heard for only a few minutes, 
yet the dogs reliably retrieved the correct toy by name afterward. 
This challenges long-held assumptions that learning from overheard speech is 
uniquely human. It suggests that some non-human animals share cognitive 
mechanisms for social word learning, pointing to deeper continuities between 
human and animal cognition than we previously assumed. It is another signal that 
we are closer to animals than many believed in the past — that our humanity is not 
detached from their animality, but connected to it through shared evolutionary roots 
of learning, communication and social understanding. The finding also underscores 
a critical point: cognitive capacities exist on a spectrum, not as a binary of “having” 
or “not having.” Gifted dogs may become powerful models for investigating the 
fundamental building blocks of communication and learning. 
But let me add a market perspective to close. The pet market is booming globally 
and is projected to grow almost 50% by the end of the decade. Imagine the potential 
for human–pet connectivity — see the comment about my personal dream below. I 
see an amazing future business opportunity. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
I need to confess: this one is personal. I discovered late in life that I truly love dogs, 
and I still miss my beloved mini-Chinouser, Lara. That is probably why this study 
struck me so deeply. 
----------------Page (14) Break----------------
We already knew that dogs can learn but this study shows that they can learn 
through passive social observation, mirroring a key stage of human language 
development. Children do not need constant instruction to acquire language. They 
listen in, scan social interactions, follow attention and gaze, and extract meaning 
from ambient speech. These dogs with exceptional vocabularies seem to do exactly 
that. That says something profound about cognition, about continuity rather than 
separation between species. 
At a time when we are witnessing rapid advances in brain–machine interfaces, here 
is my dream: I would love to connect my brain to the brains of my cats and dogs, and 
maybe others. Animals are endlessly fascinating, gentle, and emotionally rich. 
Imagine if we could communicate with them more seamlessly, beyond gestures, 
tone, or limited verbal cues. Imagine sharing meaning directly. If that future is even 
remotely possible, I want to be part of it. And if you happen to know someone 
working on that frontier, let me know. I am ready to volunteer. 
↳ Read more here 
● https://tinyurl.com/yc893edf 
● https://tinyurl.com/3787tnnn 
● https://tinyurl.com/4a9mfrvh 
● https://tinyurl.com/3ez96c47 
 A 700 million people market emerges (hopefully) 
↳ Why it matters 
Mercosur and the European Union together represent around 700 million people. The 
free trade agreement between them, which covers roughly 90% of traded goods, 
creates the largest free trade area in the world. Having the deal signed does not 
guarantee smooth or full implementation. There are safeguards, transition periods, 
and many technical details still to be worked out. But even so, this is a major step, 
particularly after 25 years of negotiation. 
----------------Page (15) Break----------------
Beyond trade flows, the agreement strengthens ties between Europe and the Global 
South at a time when the world is fragmenting. Europe is under (a lot of) economic 
and geopolitical pressure, China has made significant   advances in Latin America and 
the United States is reasserting its influence in the Western Hemisphere. In this 
context, deeper engagement with Mercosur countries becomes strategically relevant 
for Europe. 
My take is that the biggest opportunity may not be trade itself, but foreign direct 
investment. Mercosur countries can attract European capital, while Europe can 
benefit  from cost advantages in the region, especially in energy and labor–be quick, 
AI and robots will soon change this. And at a broader level, the agreement sends a 
signal that multilateralism is still possible, even as the global system we once knew 
is gone. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
The current administration in Brazil prioritized this agreement and Brazil chaired 
Mercosur until December, so there is merit to be recognized. This deal took 25 years 
to negotiate, while Brazil’s tax reform, debated for more than 35 years, was also 
finally approved in 2023. These are not changes that the average person feels 
immediately in daily life, but they are certainly relevant–the tax reform for sure, big 
time. The effects will come later. Still, in a deeply polarized and complex political 
environment, this is a display of skillful politics, domestically and across borders. 
The economic impact remains to be seen. Signing an agreement is not enough. New 
infrastructure, sustained dialogue, business ties, human resources development and 
investment mechanisms will be needed to turn potential into results. The real 
question is whether Mercosur, and Brazil in particular as its largest economy, can 
work with European partners to create new layers of cooperation that actually deliver 
outcomes. That is the challenge and the opportunity. 
----------------Page (16) Break----------------
To close: I am sure people in Brasília, Montevideo, Asunción, Buenos Aires 
(probably), Brussels, Berlin, Madrid and Rome are pleased. I am far less convinced 
that the same is true in Beijing and Washington.

Weekly Snapshot - Dec 21, 2025 
 
 Robots can dance, impress and more 
----------------Page (17) Break----------------
↳ Why it matters 
2025 started with Jensen Huang talking about robots at CES in Las Vegas. It is 
ending with 600 humanoid robots dancing in Chengdu during a live music 
performance. That arc tells you almost everything you need to know. Humanoid 
robots sit right at the intersection of AI and the physical world, and while 
performance is still uneven, the pace of progress is extraordinary. 
The real question is no longer whether demand for humanoid robots will exist, but 
when performance and price points will meet what different market segments are 
willing to adopt, which companies will succeed and which countries will dominate 
these markets. And the moment when performance and prices meet market 
requirements may come faster than many expect. Prices in China are already being 
quoted as low as US$1,200 for basic humanoid platforms. That is astonishing, and it 
immediately raises questions about sustainability, margins, long-term business 
models and national industrial strategy. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
As Elon Musk himself noted, the dancing robots in Chengdu were impressive. But the 
deeper story is not the performance, it is the industrial strategy behind it. China is 
applying the same playbook we saw in electric vehicles: dozens, sometimes 
hundreds, of companies launched at once, backed by incentives and subsidies, 
competing ferociously, driving costs down and accelerating learning curves until 
economics force consolidation. Many companies will fail. That is expected. But the 
system is designed to surface winners at speed. 
If China managed to produce roughly 70% of the world’s EVs by 2025, imagine the 
implications if it succeeds in doing something similar with humanoid robotics. This 
is not just deep tech. It is consumer-facing innovation moving fast enough to be 
visible, tangible and cultural. I was reminded of a Brazilian CEO who once told me 
she goes to China for innovation, not the U.S. What she meant was not frontier 
----------------Page (18) Break----------------
research, but the things already hitting the market and reshaping consumption. Her 
comments were about customer-facing innovation. 
Most humanoid robots may still feel experimental, even theatrical. But as an industry, 
robotics is scaling fast. Watching how countries position themselves in this race is 
essential, and it is not just China and the U.S. that are in the race. As usual, Japan is 
playing under the radar, with Toyota and other companies, and other players may 
emerge. Do not underestimate them. 2025 mattered. 2026 will matter even more. 
↳ Read more here 
https://bit.ly/4p4Wx1G 
https://bit.ly/4j90cKx 
https://bit.ly/4s7ZDVl 
https://bit.ly/4pSGwNA 
 Is the world splitting in energy strategies? 
↳ Why it matters 
The rapid expansion of AI in 2025 came with a hidden bill. New research suggests 
that AI-related activity generated CO₂ emissions comparable to those of New York 
City and consumed water volumes on the scale of the entire global bottled water 
market. At the same time, a proposed hyperscale data center in the UK’s 
Northumberland region revealed how opaque these impacts can be: estimates 
indicate that indirect water use could be up to 50 times higher than what was 
officially reported. 
----------------Page (19) Break----------------
AI is no longer just a digital phenomenon. It is a material force with real 
environmental externalities. Data centers already represent the fastest-growing 
category of energy consumption worldwide. Around 45% of them are located in the 
United States, followed by the European Union with roughly 15%. Feeding this growth 
has become a strategic question. In parallel, 2025 saw a surge of announcements in 
the U.S. around nuclear power for AI, from small modular reactors to renewed 
interest in fusion. 
Meanwhile, China massively expanded solar and wind generation, scaled clean-tech 
manufacturing and flooded global markets with hardware, materials and systems. 
Science magazine recently named the surge in renewables the scientific   
breakthrough of 2025, noting that renewables surpassed coal as the world’s largest 
source of electricity and that solar and wind alone covered the entire growth in global 
electricity demand in the first half of the year. Most of that capacity came from 
Chinese manufacturing. 
This makes one thing clear: the AI conversation is not just about algorithms, and not 
even just about the computer chips on which algorithms run. It is about energy 
systems, industrial capacity, global trade and the ability of states to align industrial 
policy, with technology at the center, and business strategies at scale. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
What recent news about AI exposes is the environmental cost it carries, but it also 
suggests the potential emergence of two distinct energy strategies to cope with AI 
needs. In the West, and especially in the U.S., the response to AI’s energy hunger is 
increasingly nuclear-centric. In China, the biggest emitter today, the overarching 
energy focus has been to dominate clean tech across solar, wind, batteries, grids, 
materials and exports, while keeping nuclear and fusion largely out of Western 
headlines. 
----------------Page (20) Break----------------
That raises some deep questions. Has the U.S. effectively ceded leadership in clean 
tech manufacturing? And what is China doing in nuclear, small reactors and fusion 
that we are not seeing or not discussing? The Chinese model is playing out in full 
view: coordinated industrial policy, subsidies, intense internal competition, global 
market focus and relentless scaling. It worked for EVs. It is now shaping global clean 
energy. 
The U.S. has clearly awakened to this reality and is experimenting with new policy 
tools under the Trump administration. But the real elephant in the room is Europe. Is 
Europe equipped to play this game at all? Can it position itself between the U.S. and 
China with a coherent energy, industrial and AI strategy? Or is the European model, 
as it stands, simply crumbling under pressure? 
One thing I will be watching closely in 2026 is whether anything genuinely new 
emerges from Europe. Because in a world where AI, energy and electrification   are 
converging, sitting on the sidelines is no longer an option. 
↳ Read more here 
https://bit.ly/4p2Gul7 
https://bit.ly/4pOTgEO 
https://bit.ly/3MPciMP 
https://bit.ly/4asAzlz 
 China runs another large-scale economic 
experiment 
↳ Why it matters 
----------------Page (21) Break----------------
Deng Xiaoping and Rajiv Gandhi allegedly coined the expression “the century of 
Asia.” In 2025, 60% of global growth came from Asia. As anticipated at the beginning 
of the year, the Chinese government launched the rules for the new economic regime 
for Hainan. Hainan is the southern part of China, a tropical island. If it were an 
independent country, it would be the 70th largest economy in the world. 
What China has done is impressive, lifting hundreds of millions of people out of 
poverty and advancing its scientific   and industrial economy. But this has not come 
without challenges. We have all read about the importance the Chinese government 
places on energizing the domestic market. But they have not succeeded yet. And, as 
expected, growth has slowed. China still needs to grow. 
So how can growth be sustained in China? It is impossible to replicate the Chinese 
model. But what China is doing in Hainan is creating a new gateway to China. And in 
some sense, this is a major experiment, one that is only possible in China. But it will 
force other countries to reposition themselves and, once again, create a new 
economic space. 
Very importantly, what has been done is not only about trade in goods and services. 
There is also, and for some time now, a very important digital component. 
Companies and people in Hainan can operate outside the Great Firewall. The 
meaning and scale of this are even greater. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
China has a unique reality, unique possibilities and unique challenges. It can 
generate large-scale experiments like the one in Hainan. China has many resorts and 
beaches, but the implementation of this new free trade economic regime is a major 
experiment. Other countries cannot even think about doing this. 
A hypothesis that has circulated for a long time is the “China peak” hypothesis, the 
idea that China’s economy has already peaked. There are major challenges in terms 
----------------Page (22) Break----------------
of economic dynamics, internal growth, the health of the banking system, questions 
around real estate bubbles and, above all, China’s ability to continue growing. 
Population decline is at the center of this. 
With Hainan, China is forcing other countries to change and reposition themselves–I 
strongly encourage everyone to read Alex Lin’s analysis; follow the links below for 
that. But the bigger question is whether China, by generating large-scale 
experiments, will be able to change some basic and fundamental dynamics, such as 
those related to population and demographic trends. That remains to be seen. 
As we close the first quarter of the 21st century, there is still a long road ahead. And 
Asia will be at the center of many of the developments we will see for the rest of the 
century. We all should know much more about Asia and China than we do today. By 
the way... I’ve never been to Hainan, have you? 
↳ Read more here

Weekly Snapshot - Dec 14, 2025 
 
 Between a rock and a hard place 
↳ Why it matters 
In today’s world, balancing relationships with the two big powers is not easy. 
Geography matters, and for Mexico and Canada, more than for most others, dancing 
with the U.S. is destiny. Both are deeply tied to the United States, but each is 
adjusting its strategy in response to a more fragmented and complex global 
landscape. Both are in motion, flexing their capabilities, testing limits, experimenting 
and generating lessons. 
Mexico’s decision to raise tariffs on Chinese and other Asian imports is more than a 
trade move. It signals alignment with U.S. concerns over supply chains and industrial 
competition, reinforced by visible improvements in security and a stronger state 
response to organized crime — and Batman is a great nickname, isn’t it? Together, 
these steps help stabilize and strengthen Mexico’s relationship with Washington. 
Canada is taking a different route. It wants to expand exports and reduce 
dependence on the U.S. by deepening ties with Asia, including China, while still 
----------------Page (24) Break----------------
preserving a functional and trusted relationship with its southern neighbor. Managing 
both at once is complex and can be risky. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
As I’ve written before, strategic capabilities are in short supply across the world. 
Dealing with internal challenges while positioning a country in a turbulent global 
system demands coordination, experience, political clarity and resources. Mexico 
and Canada illustrate both the possibilities and the limits of this balancing act. 
Something that clearly stands out is a shift away from ideology toward pragmatism. 
For countries caught between great powers, this is less about picking sides and 
more about building the capacity to navigate uncertainty and a fussy environment. 
Others should watch closely and learn.

 2025 was the AI year, get ready for what will come 
next 
↳ Why it matters 
This week, TIME named “The Architects of AI” as its 2025 Person of the Year, not a 
single individual, but the engineers, researchers and executives shaping the AI 
revolution. It is a symbolic but powerful signal: artificial intelligence is not a 
supporting actor in economic and geopolitical narratives. It is the protagonist and 
the storyline itself. From productivity gains and scientific   breakthroughs to labor 
displacement and power concentration, AI is increasingly influencing how 
----------------Page (25) Break----------------
economies grow, how companies compete and how governments think about 
regulation, security and sovereignty. 
The obvious implications are that (1) AI is and will be everywhere — we will soon 
have two types of companies: those that use AI and those that closed their doors — 
and (2) all nations, cities and governments need practical AI strategies, not just loose 
papers and vague declarations. Beyond that, and very importantly, the “AI age” also 
signals a major shift of power from nation-states to industry, like never before, not 
just in AI. Think of SpaceX and space, for instance. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
Two weeks ago, I wrote here about the U.S. Genesis Mission, highlighting the central 
role that AI has in that and the U.S. Government ambitions related to it. The scale of 
today’s AI push is unprecedented, specially when compared to past endeavors and 
technology shifts. At their peaks, the Manhattan Project absorbed about 0.4% of U.S. 
GDP and Apollo about 0.8%. Investments in AI in 2025 alone exceeded 1.3%. Nothing 
in modern history compares to the volume of capital, infrastructure, energy and 
talent now flowing into AI worldwide. And I bet this is not just in the U.S. 
An MIT report recently described this AI moment as an “iceberg,” when we think 
about the impacts on the economy. What we see today is only the tip. Beneath the 
surface, AI will have much deeper impacts on jobs, economic structures and 
societies. If 2025 was the year of AI, the years ahead will be about its consequences. 
Productivity gains are already impressive and, from personal experience, what AI can 
do today is astonishing. But disruption is coming at scale. Many people will be 
surprised, many will lose their jobs and our institutions are not ready. In the U.S., only 
43% of young people have a positive view of capitalism. Globally, Gen Z protests are 
rising, like we saw this past week in Bulgaria. When rapid technological change 
meets social fragility, tensions grow. AI will create enormous value, but it will also 
test societies in ways we may not be prepared for. 
----------------Page (26) Break----------------
↳ Read more here

https://bit.ly/4q3kHKQ

 Chile makes a right turn 
↳ Why it matters 
Latin America has the highest cost of security as a share of GDP in the world, and 
Chile’s recent election put that reality front and center. Crime and migration 
dominated the campaign. With Venezuela collapsing and economies lagging behind 
in parts of the region, such as Bolivia, Chile has seen a dramatic surge in migration: 
the migrant population is rising and the number of undocumented migrants has 
grown tenfold in just five years. That is a massive shock for any society. 
The election result marks a clear shift to the right, echoing broader regional trends 
after Milei’s election in Argentina, where tougher stances on security are gaining 
traction while the left struggles to articulate credible answers. This will not stop with 
Chile. Security will shape politics across Latin America, including Brazil’s 2026 
election. Can the left have an answer to the security crisis? 
↳ My thoughts on it 
Chile is a strikingly beautiful country, a thin ribbon between the Andes and the 
Pacific,  with Santiago framed by mountains and a dynamic business district, but with 
deep regional disparities. It has made real progress, reaching a GDP per capita of 
roughly $17–18k, yet inequality remains high and growth has slowed. Those 
tensions erupted in the 2019 protests and later in a failed constitutional reform, 
which also exposed the limits of identity-driven politics detached from economic 
----------------Page (27) Break----------------
realities. Kast, the president-elect, promises fiscal cuts without sacrificing   social 
progress, an ambitious balance that remains untested. 
Crime, while rising sharply in the north, is still lower than in much of the region, a 
reminder of how grim Latin America’s baseline has become — for comparison, 
murder rates in northern Chile are below the Brazilian average, which, by the way, has 
reached its lowest point in 11 years. Enormous resources continue to be absorbed 
by the fight against drugs, even as the war on drugs has clearly failed across the 
continent. Demand persists, supply adapts and societies pay the price. 
Chile’s economy still depends heavily on copper, nearly 60% of exports, a blessing as 
global demand surges but also a constraint. Despite strong talent and investment in 
science, Chile has not become a global innovation powerhouse. What is holding it 
back? That question matters not just for Chile, but for a region searching for a way to 
deliver security, opportunity and dignity at the same time.

Weekly Snapshot - Dec 07, 2025 
 
 Rethinking rocket science 
↳ Why it matters 
The space economy is growing fast and, according to McKinsey, it will reach $1.8 
trillion per year by 2035. SpaceX has transformed the economics of launch, and it is 
slated to do it again with the Heavy. But a lot more is needed. Space is also getting 
crowded and we will need to launch many things into space, and we will need to 
make and do many things in space. We need to grow food, build things, manufacture, 
store data, process data and so on. And this means we will need a diversity of 
solutions, not just one type of solution. 
With Moonshot Space coming out of stealth mode this past week, it adds to the cast 
of companies experimenting with alternative launch systems. As reported, they will 
use electromagnetism to accelerate and launch payloads into space. But not all 
types of payloads, especially those less sensitive to acceleration and impact, like 
supplies, raw materials, components and parts, but not sophisticated electronics 
such as satellites. 
This development is also relevant because it is a clear spinoff of Israel’s defense 
investments. The CEO was a former key leader in the Iron Dome project, the 
tremendously successful air defense system Israel put in place to protect the 
country from threats flying in from hostile neighbors. All combined, this is a big 
display of the opportunity for alternative launch systems, the spinoffs from Israel’s 
defense programs and Israel’s amazing entrepreneurship drive. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
----------------Page (29) Break----------------
At Seldor Capital, we invested in a tremendously efficient and innovative Hyperloop 
company, Swisspod. That company has a brilliant team and they just had a very 
successful Hyperloop Day in Pueblo, Colorado. But you may be thinking what a 
space tech fund was doing investing in Hyperloop, right? Well, there are two 
answers. The first is that Hyperloop is a super cool technology, and they have a 
tremendously creative, technically sound and highly effective team. The other 
answer is that we envisioned the type of thing that Moonshot Space aims to do. We 
see the need for diverse solutions to enable the space economy, including the 
opportunity for alternative launch systems to put stuff into space. 
But there is something else here. An important part of the economic rationale for 
Moonshot Space is that when a rocket is launched, the payload is actually a tiny 
fraction of the weight, the mass that is accelerated into space. This means we use a 
lot of energy to move things just to move a small quantity of materials up there, so 
efficiency is very low. This reminds me of another mobility solution that most people 
around the world have never heard about: the AeroMobile or AeroM system, which is 
a wind-propelled rail system. This means that train wagons do not have drivetrains, 
batteries or engines. The propulsion system is outside the vehicle, and this makes 
the whole system much more efficient because you do not need to waste energy to 
accelerate dead weight, something you do not really need to move. The same logic 
applies to the launch system that Moonshot Space is building. It is incredibly 
interesting to see how Earth mobility systems are conceptually connected to what 
they are doing, isn’t it? 
It is very interesting to see, and it will be very interesting to follow what comes next 
with Moonshot Space and others. And for you all on Earth, stay tuned on Swisspod 
and Aerom. 
↳ Read more here 
● https://tinyurl.com/429svrbt 
● https://tinyurl.com/58zzd554

● https://tinyurl.com/2kd3jhua 
 Nature is disappearing 
↳ Why it matters 
A recent study published in Nature estimates that 80% of mammal biomass in Africa 
has already vanished. Another study, tracking over 5,000 vertebrate species, shows a 
73% decline in their populations between 1970 and 2020. That’s almost three out of 
every four animals — gone. These are not abstract numbers. We are losing things we 
don’t even know exist. Insects. Plant species. Fungi. Life forms that have evolved 
over millions of years are disappearing silently. Every extinction is a lost opportunity 
— for food, for medicine, for inspiration, for balance. 
But this is more than biodiversity. We are dismantling the infrastructure of life on 
Earth. Forests, soils, rivers, wetlands, oceans — they are not just “nature.” They are 
our food system, our climate regulation, our water supply, our health, our prosperity. 
These ecosystems provide services that we depend on every single day. Without 
them, everything else collapses. And it’s also personal. To some of us–like me, 
nature is sacred. It is not just resource — it is beauty, balance, and existence itself. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
I remember going to the movies as a kid in the 1970s, watching wide-eyed as lions 
roamed the savannas of Africa and whales leapt across the screen. I remember that 
sense of wonder. Children love animals. They love forests and rivers and birds and 
turtles. So when is it that we lose that? At what age does death and destruction 
become normal? When do we stop marveling at life and start accepting its 
disappearance as inevitable? 
There’s something deeply broken when we speak of GDP growth while forests burn, 
or when we talk about innovation while species vanish forever. Because ultimately, 
----------------Page (31) Break----------------
nature is not “out there.” It is us. That’s not just a philosophical question. If we 
normalize the collapse of nature, we lose more than species — we lose our humanity. 
↳ Read more here 
● https://tinyurl.com/ttzhc6s2 
● https://tinyurl.com/2mk57hef 
● https://tinyurl.com/2s459897 
 Give peace a chance 
↳ Why it matters 
Pope Leo’s visit to Lebanon last week is not just a gesture — it is a powerful bet on 
reconciliation. Lebanon has suffered immensely. From the civil war to Hezbollah’s 
conflicts  with Israel, the country’s economy has collapsed, poverty has soared and 
its society was brought to its knees. From a golden age in the 1960s and a beautiful 
past, Lebanon became a symbol of despair with a crumbling economy, a 
non-existent internal financial market, a large diaspora and remittances 
corresponding to more than 50% of GDP. 
Beirut has always been a crossroads where cultures, religions, people and tribes 
meet. For far too long, the Middle East has been shaken by violence. And I really 
mean for far too long. Waves of invasions from the East and the West — from every 
side — have defined  the region’s history for millennia. And Lebanon can only be 
understood within a broader regional context, and the region only within a broader 
global one. 
The Levant is one of the cradles of Western civilization. If Lebanon can be fixed, if its 
society can heal and connections be mended, maybe it can offer a template for other 
places in the world. 
----------------Page (32) Break----------------
↳ My thoughts on it 
Beirut was one of the most beautiful cities on Earth. Before the civil war, it was. 
Going there after the war was something else entirely. I had never seen a city where 
the buildings looked like Swiss cheese — walls torn by bullets and bombs. And that 
was 20 years after the war had ended. But the geography is stunning, and the people 
are extraordinary. I have many Lebanese (and Lebanese Brazilian, Lebanese 
American, etc.) friends, and I admire them deeply — their resilience, their culture, 
their warmth. 
The complexity of that society is gigantic and fascinating. I remember sitting in a 
hotel lobby in Beirut and seeing the diversity of people coming and going, feeling like 
I had walked into a James Bond movie. People in corners wearing business, military, 
religious and ethnic attire, intense conversations, quiet signals, excitement and 
mistery — something was in the air. 
Maybe Pope Leo’s visit is the spark that reminds us all that peace is a process that 
requires outreach, not a miracle. Good signals are popping up, such as the 
conversations with Israel moving forward. It is never too late for hope. And it is never 
too late for the Pope.

Weekly Snapshot - Nov 30, 2025 
 
 Turbulent times require bold approaches. 
↳ Why it matters 
The White House just announced Genesis Mission, a sweeping national initiative that 
will leverage capabilities, datasets and resources — for example, the supercomputers 
owned and operated by the U.S. national labs — to accelerate breakthroughs in 
biotechnology, advanced energy, critical materials, advanced manufacturing, 
semiconductors, next-generation quantum computing and beyond. It is not a single 
program, it is a whole-of-government effort designed to align federal agencies, 
industry, academia and civil society around long-term innovation goals. More 
importantly, from a conceptual standpoint, it is designed to surf the “AI as a 
scientist” (and engineer) trend I have commented on in this Snapshot on past 
occasions. 
Throughout history, the U.S. has shown its ability to mobilize resources at scale to 
address major challenges — think of the Manhattan Project, the Apollo Program, 
DARPA’s moonshots and others. Genesis is a national mission to accelerate science 
through artificial intelligence and has a fundamental characteristic: it is a program 
with massive resources but not a massive budget per se. Intrigued? Genesis will 
connect, coordinate, leverage and create value from resources that, for the most 
part, are already there — existing datasets, the supercomputers that the national labs 
----------------Page (34) Break----------------
have, and scientific   and innovation capabilities distributed across government 
agencies, universities and industry. This highlights two fundamental things at the 
same time: the importance of bold projects and the imperative, along with the 
opportunities to unleash value creation and impact, of coordinating initiatives and 
resources. Other countries should learn from that, whatever the results are. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
From the pyramids, the Wall of China, and the road and water systems in the Roman 
Empire to the massive technology projects of the 20th and 21st centuries, broad, 
ambitious and transformational technology initiatives drive economic change, 
launch new industries and push economies and humanity into the future. They act as 
focusing mechanisms, compress time, concentrate talent and unlock invention that 
markets alone would take decades to reach. We extensively reviewed 
transformational technology projects and made the case for them in the GFCC — 
follow the links below for the Leveraging Extreme Innovation report about that. 
But transformational technology projects require three things: resources, leadership 
and coordination. Their importance can hardly be overemphasized, and I certainly 
have my own country in mind when I make this comment. Bold initiatives need to be 
coordinated at or from — or in close coordination with — the center of power, the 
prime minister or the president's office. In the U.S., the DoE is leading the initiative in 
coordination with the White House Office  of Science and Technology Policy. 
Importantly, countries need leadership (yes, I am repeating this), vision and 
institutional infrastructures to enable all of that. 
Let's think about the practicalities of this and the possibilities of the U.S. model 
being replicated in other environments (the model, not the resources). To catalyze 
thinking, I will leave you with a few questions. We are living in the digital era, right? So 
which countries in the world have a data strategy for government-funded research? 
Which countries have directories to standardize data collection, storage and 
----------------Page (35) Break----------------
treatment of research endeavors and results? Just think about it. And tell me if 
anyone comes to your mind. 
↳ Read more here 
● https://tinyurl.com/328yv27u 
● https://tinyurl.com/258h7wky 
● https://tinyurl.com/mvfmd8tk 
● https://tinyurl.com/rx8mv56e 
 New partnerships are emerging in the world 
↳ Why it matters 
This past week, Kazakhstan and the UAE announced a deeper strategic partnership 
focused on high-tech cooperation, investment and long-term industrial development. 
On the surface, it may look like an extended regional economic story, but it is more 
than that. 
Geographically, Kazakhstan sits at the crossroads of Russia, China and India. That is 
a part of the world that has shaped global trade for thousands of years, from the Silk 
Road to today’s energy and logistics corridors. Add the U.S. to the equation and, 
metaphorically, Kazakhstan is at an even more complex crossroad. Positioning the 
country among all these big players in what the media in Astana calls a “multi-vector 
policy” is not a trivial task. Only a handful of countries can aim to strike a balance. 
Which are those? 
One of the first countries that comes to my mind because of its strategic capabilities 
and its ability to successfully balance relationships with the so-called West, China 
and Russia is the UAE. But the UAE has also become a global investment 
powerhouse, deploying capital with long-term strategic intent far beyond the Middle 
East. Think of Mubadala’s footprint in Brazil or ADQ’s growing influence across Asia 
----------------Page (36) Break----------------
and Africa. When the UAE invests, it is rarely transactional, it is part of a broader 
blueprint to access critical resources, form long-term bonds and shape the industries 
and technologies of the future. 
If Kazakhstan needs a template to successfully navigate the complexities of today's 
world, the UAE can be a good one. There is a lot of space for institutional learning. 
Bonus track: access to capital, always important, especially for a country in between 
with about $10,000 of GDP per capita, and a shared ambition to move from a natural 
resources-based economy to the innovation economy. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
I’ve seen innovation on the ground in Kazakhstan, and it surprised me. Some of the 
most interesting models I’ve encountered in the world for driving innovation outside 
traditional sectors like mining were built there, combining the demand for hardware 
and software with global research, industry and tech investment partnerships. A 
good friend tells me the most “digital” construction and real estate company he has 
seen in the whole world is there. 
The UAE accumulated wealth and is using it to construct the foundations of a future 
economy driven by technology and innovation, not oil. It has also become a major 
global business hub, from finance to entertainment (think of the amusement parks in 
Abu Dhabi; by the way, Kazakhstan attracts a lot of wealthy Indian families for 
weddings... kind of amusement too, right?), and obviously digital, crypto, AI and even 
space and biotech, and a magnet for HNWIs and ambitious entrepreneurs. The UAE 
is not just building its future, it is increasingly shaping innovation trajectories 
globally. 
In spite of the differences in wealth and the challenges each nation faces, it is 
fascinating to see Kazakhstan and the UAE further connecting. In different ways and 
with different levels of resource allocation, both nations are striving to advance their 
innovation capabilities. One is extending its strategic reach, and the other needs a 
----------------Page (37) Break----------------
template and partners to avoid being boxed by the big powers. While most of the 
world is not paying attention, a ramification  of a new, ideology-agnostic and 
pragmatic global axis may be shaping up. Never forget: many (most?) of the big 
business opportunities in the world may be in our blindspots. 
↳ Read more here

● https://tinyurl.com/23cucaex

 Nuclear fusion meets biology in the global South 
↳ Why it matters 
While much of the global conversation on energy transition focuses on innovative 
hardware and software solutions to enable advanced nuclear, next-gen solar, 
grid-scale batteries and hydrogen, biomass remains a mature, low-cost and scalable 
technology to produce renewable energy. This is particularly relevant for countries 
with abundant organic material, agricultural waste or favorable climates. In 
Southeast Asia, recent debates suggest that biomass-based energy generation may 
double in the years to come, with a priority focus on industry utilization. 
And in a world hungry for energy, with climate goals increasingly difficult  to reach, 
biomass offers an alternative and complementary pathway to other emerging 
technologies and an avenue of opportunities that is mostly open for countries in the 
southern part of the globe. With land, water, sunlight and chlorophyll, they have an 
advantage in photosynthesis capacity. For those countries, biomass and bioenergy 
can combine the unique advantages given by location and natural resources with the 
development of indigenous technology, agricultural and mnufacturing capabilities, 
business and whole industry sectors, as Brazil has done in the case of ethanol and 
biodiesel. Countries in the South do not need to follow the paths chosen by those in 
----------------Page (38) Break----------------
the North and, in doing so, they can develop competitive advantages. The window of 
opportunity, however, will not stay open forever and timing will be critical. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
The best energy-storage technology humanity has ever seen is photosynthesis. The 
energy coming from the sun — originating via a nuclear fusion process — is 
transformed into glucose and stored in biomass through a process that captures and 
stores carbon. And the regions with the greatest natural endowment for this are 
mostly in the southern part of the planet — Southeast Asia, Latin America and Africa. 
The potential for everything biomass-related is immense in those areas. 
Brazil is the undisputed champion of bioenergy in the world, both because of the 
unparalleled total capacity that plants in the country have to absorb carbon dioxide 
from the atmosphere through photosynthesis to produce energy and because of the 
investments the country made to create and consolidate a vigorous bioenergy 
industry sector. In the 1970s, without money to import oil, it built an entire ethanol 
industry from scratch. For decades, Brazilian cars ran on ethanol and a complete 
biomanufacturing ecosystem emerged. 
However, Brazil never fully internationalized its biofuels industry as it could. But it 
still can, especially when modern biotechnology unlocks even more value from 
biomass and new technologies such as those to manufacture hydrogen from ethanol 
emerge and mature. There is also enormous potential for global connections and 
cross-border deals, as companies such as Cemvita, Be8 and Sumitomo illustrate, 
connecting industrial processes with biological innovation to turn biomass into 
high-value products taken to global markets. This should also be an inspiring case 
for Malaysia, Indonesia and others. 
↳ Read more here 
● https://tinyurl.com/bdznzejf 
----------------Page (39) Break----------------
● https://tinyurl.com/47vcxwrn

Weekly Snapshot - Nov 23, 2025 
 
 A key player in today's global chess 
↳ Why it matters 
Last Tuesday, the White House unveiled a sweeping framework to deepen 
U.S.–Saudi cooperation across defense, energy, technology and investment. Crown 
Prince Mohammed bin Salman (MBS) added fuel to this by confirming  plans for the 
Kingdom to invest up to $1 trillion in the United States. This is a lot of money, even in 
and for the U.S. Riyadh wants to anchor its Vision 2030 transformation to the world’s 
most dynamic innovation and financial ecosystem, while Washington wants access 
to Saudi Arabia's capital and to bring it closer to its sphere at a moment of 
intensifying global competition. But there is more behind the scenes. 
----------------Page (40) Break----------------
In a shifting global landscape, with uncertainty about the long-term U.S. debt 
trajectory and pressure on the U.S. dollar as a global currency, especially as China 
could accelerate the sale of U.S. bonds, it is critical to count on Middle Eastern 
countries to hold these assets. But there is more. Nations in the Middle East, 
particularly the UAE, Saudi Arabia and Israel, have become in different ways central 
players in the future of advanced technologies. Peace in the region is essential not 
only to relieve suffering and stop the carnage but also to accelerate its rise as a key 
engine of the global economy and the future of technology. The emerging 
U.S.–Saudi agreement, or its current expression of intent, shows how important 
Middle Eastern countries have become for the future of the global economy and 
technology. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
The trillion-dollar commitment changes the psychology of the U.S.–Saudi 
relationship. It turns Riyadh into one of the most significant   foreign investors in U.S. 
strategic industries and gives Saudi Arabia access, leverage and technological 
proximity it would not get anywhere else. But where will the money come from? 
Saudi Arabia's Public Investment Fund (PIF) is the country's sovereign wealth fund 
established in 1971. It is one of the world's largest sovereign wealth funds and 
serves as the main engine for Saudi Arabia's Vision 2030, investing in a wide range of 
domestic and international opportunities, including strategic sectors and large-scale 
giga-projects within the Kingdom, and also makes significant   global investments. 
While PIF projects an image of massive wealth and ambition, liquidity challenges and 
underperforming projects — think of NEOM and other giga-projects — reveal 
constraints. PIF’s assets (~$1 trillion) are largely tied up in illiquid projects, limiting 
immediate capital for new deals. Behind the scenes, the fund is restructuring 
operations, cutting projections and changing leadership in troubled projects. 
Realignments are natural and the Kingdom will continue to generate free cash from 
its energy exports. However, the scenario suggests that Western partners should be 
----------------Page (41) Break----------------
increasingly prepared to also deploy capital in the Kingdom, not just draw resources 
from there. Like him or not, MBS is driving a major transformation in Saudi Arabia. It 
is not an isolated case, as the UAE shows. This brings to the table a fundamental 
and uncomfortable question for Western democracies: can they succeed in building 
and executing strategic visions to transform themselves as countries in the Middle 
East are? 
While China continues expanding its footprint in the Gulf, Washington is doubling 
down on Riyadh. The Middle East is once again becoming a battlefield   of influence, 
but the game is different this time. The next decade of geopolitics and tech will not 
be a tedious one. 
↳ Read more here 
https://bit.ly/49vWyYq

 Quantum: Japan bets big on optical computation 
↳ Why it matters 
NTT and OptQC have signed an agreement to build a 1-million-qubit optical quantum 
computer by 2030, a scale previously considered closer to science fiction than 
engineering reality. Optical approaches promise higher stability, lower error rates and 
more scalability than traditional superconducting designs. The announcement 
bridges two areas that are experiencing progress and for which there are high 
expectations — quantum and optical computation — across the world. If achieved, 
this would be a big feat. A 1-million-qubit quantum computer would radically change 
what is computationally possible: molecular simulations at atomic accuracy, 
real-time global logistics optimisation, unbreakable encryption and AI models that 
----------------Page (42) Break----------------
make today’s frontier systems look small. And it would signal something bigger — 
we would be moving from “quantum promise” to “quantum industrialisation.” 
However, optical computation still is in its early days and the promise of functional 
quantum computing has been a moving target for quite some time. Anyone 
interested in the theme and the potential impacts these technologies can have must 
carefully follow the technical advancements that may arise from the initiative. But 
there is more here. Having two Japanese companies pushing this forward is a major 
thing. Japan has lost its leadership position in semiconductors. If successful, this 
initiative could open new frontiers for Japanese competitiveness and global 
leadership. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
To be realized, this feat will require technology developments and even 
breakthroughs in different domains, but the two Japanese players seem to be well 
positioned for the challenge. OptQC has successfully developed the world’s first new 
optical quantum computer that operates at room temperature and pressure. In 
January 2025, researchers at NTT, the Graduate School of Engineering at the 
University of Tokyo and the National Institute of Information and Communications 
Technology realized the world’s first general-purpose optical quantum computing 
platform capable of performing calculations using optical pulses with strong 
quantum properties. 
At the same time, we are seeing a surge in optical computing research across the 
world. Here's an example: about three months ago, Arago, a hyped Paris-based 
startup, announced a $26 million seed round with investors from the U.S. and 
Europe. They plan to take to the market an optical computer chip and a stack of 
software solutions to seamlessly integrate those with existing computer systems. 
Quantum has lived in the realm of prototypes, not platforms. What I find most 
fascinating is the shift from “let’s explore the physics” to “let’s engineer the 
----------------Page (43) Break----------------
roadmap.” If they deliver even half of this, the quantum race will look very different by 
the end of the decade. Make your bets. 
↳ Read more here 
https://bit.ly/49CyEKP 
https://bit.ly/4ojoS43 
https://bit.ly/4psKFqS 
 Europe: can it unleash its deep-tech potential? 
↳ Why it matters 
A new analysis shows that European deep-tech and life-sciences spinouts now reach 
a combined valuation of $398 billion, driven by university–industry pipelines and a 
surge in frontier-tech investment. From quantum and synthetic biology to med-tech 
and advanced materials, Europe’s innovation ecosystems are scaling faster and 
more seriously than many expected. This is not about chasing Silicon Valley. It is 
about Europe building its own innovation architecture rooted in research density, 
strong public funding and rising private deep-tech capital. Although a big gap with 
the U.S. and China persists, Europe certainly has a chance in a world increasingly 
dominated by disputes over critical, science-intensive technologies. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
Europe often gets framed as “slow” in tech, but that thinking misses a critical point: 
deep-tech is where the continent excels and could build an edge. Strong universities, 
a highly educated and specialised talent pool and the availability of long-term capital 
make Europe one of the best places in the world for science-driven companies to 
emerge. The big issue is whether they have a business environment that is 
conducive to growth. One missing piece is venture capital — the percentage of VC 
----------------Page (44) Break----------------
capital invested in Europe as a fraction of total global VC dollars is less than 
Europe's share of global GDP; by contrast, the global share of VC capital invested in 
the U.S. is more than twice its share of global GDP. This gap is even more 
pronounced in later stages. To compound the situation, IPOs are much more limited 
in Europe (think London, for instance) than in the U.S. 
Something that stands out in Europe is the diversification  of deep-tech and 
asset-heavy areas in which the continent has strong technical capabilities: quantum, 
nuclear, biotech, advanced materials, space, neurotech, robotics and others. The 
foundations are there and the bottlenecks of Europe’s competitiveness are mostly 
known, as the Draghi report unveiled. If Europe succeeds in advancing 
commercialisation and funding scale in a market-driven way, historically its weak 
points, this $398 billion could be just the beginning.

 Drinks are changing... and digital may make a 
difference 
↳ Why it matters 
The global coffee shop market is massive—valued between $70 and $220 
billion—and growing fast with a 7.1% CAGR. The growing participation of Chinese 
coffee shop (and tea) retail brands in the U.S. and other markets is not just about 
good money, it’s a sign of changing lifestyles. For years, Starbucks symbolized global 
coffee culture, expanding aggressively in China, at one point opening a new store 
every day. Now things are shifting. Starbucks is reportedly selling a major stake in its 
China business to a private equity fund, while Chinese coffee brands such as Luckin 
Coffee, Heytea, Chagee, and Mixue are entering the U.S. market. And they are doing 
so with a different playbook: digital first, lower touch, and flavor forward. This is not 
just about coffee. It is about how consumer habits, brand preferences, and cultural 
exports are changing. And about who wins in this new phase. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
It started with small electronics, phones, PCs, and EVs. Brands like Geely, Lenovo, 
Huawei, Haier, and BYD were barely known globally ten years ago. Now they’re 
everywhere—especially in the Global South. Ask young people in Africa what phone 
or car they want, and odds are it’s Chinese. And European brands? Largely out of the 
----------------Page (46) Break----------------
picture. Now it is coffee and beverages. What is new is that Chinese consumer 
brands are entering the most competitive retail market in the world: the U.S. They’re 
not just producing goods; they’re building experiences and aiming to capture hearts, 
taste buds, and loyalty, especially from younger generations. So here’s the big 
question: Are Chinese brands more digitally ready and culturally tuned to win Gen Z 
and Gen Alpha than legacy Western names in food, retail, and lifestyle? To close: 
have you thought about the day when you will crave a Chinese burger?

 AI started to discover... and it is just getting started 
↳ Why it matters 
A paper in Nature released this past week shows that researchers in China have 
introduced AI-Newton, a system capable of discovering physical laws directly from 
raw experimental data with no human guidance. It analyzes messy observations, 
generates hypotheses, rules out inconsistent explanations, and converges on 
equations that describe the real world, including versions of Newton’s second law. 
Also this past week, Edison, a company in San Francisco spun off from Future 
House, a nonprofit dedicated to building AI agents to automate research, released 
Kosmos, an “AI Scientist” for autonomous discovery. Science is changing for good 
and for real, in real time. 
This marks a turning point: AI moves from being a prediction tool to becoming a 
scientific   discovery engine, potentially uncovering hidden structures in nature and 
accelerating breakthroughs that would traditionally take years of human work. And 
----------------Page (47) Break----------------
science is one of the highest-level human activities, and AI will transform it like 
everything else. Scientific   discovery can also accelerate—Edison claims that a single 
Kosmos run can accomplish work equivalent to six months of a PhD or postdoctoral 
scientist. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
We have long assumed that science—forming hypotheses, abstract reasoning, 
intuition—was a uniquely human domain. But if an AI can infer core physical 
principles from scratch, we may be witnessing the rise of a new kind of scientist: 
nonhuman, tireless, and capable of exploring theoretical spaces we have not even 
imagined, conceptualizing experiments, and processing experimental data at 
unparalleled speed. 
The question shifts from “What can AI learn?” to “What can AI reveal that we don’t 
yet know how to ask?” And... “What will scientists do?” This reframes everything: 
validation, regulation, and the very role of human researchers. If machines start 
generating hypotheses at scale, our job may increasingly be to interrogate and 
interpret what they uncover. And it is not far-fetched to imagine that the next major 
scientific   leap will not originate from a university lab, but from a model running 
quietly on a cluster somewhere in China or in San Francisco. 
↳ Read more here 
https://bit.ly/4nYCcKK

 Choose a location to call your own (and secure, 
hopefully) 
----------------Page (48) Break----------------
↳ Why it matters 
A new Gartner survey shows that 61% of CIOs and IT leaders in Western Europe plan 
to shift toward local or regional cloud providers because of rising geopolitical risks. 
Concerns around digital sovereignty, data jurisdiction, and foreign dependencies are 
pushing enterprises to rethink their cloud architecture. This is not just about security, 
it is a structural shift in how organizations choose infrastructure. Global hyperscalers 
may remain dominant, but governments and regulated industries increasingly want 
clouds they can politically trust, not only technically rely on. Geopolitics has become 
dominant, at least for those who have the strategic capabilities and the space to 
maneuver–most countries don't. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
We talk a lot about chips, AI models and data pipelines, but geopolitics is quietly 
reshaping the foundations underneath all of those. As more countries begin to treat 
cloud infrastructure as a strategic national asset, the entire AI ecosystem must 
adjust—model deployment, data residency, compliance, and vendor selection. For 
companies building AI-first products, this raises new questions: where does your 
model run and under whose jurisdiction, can your stack be deployed regionally 
without losing performance, and how do you prove sovereignty, not just security. 
Cloud sovereignty is becoming the new competitive frontier. In the coming years, 
global by default may give way to regional by design, and those who adapt early will 
define  the next wave of trusted AI infrastructure.
