 India aims big; other nations should watch closely 
↳ Why it matters 
This past week, NVIDIA joined India’s Deep Tech Alliance, an ambitious national 
effort to supercharge innovation in AI, semiconductors, advanced computing, 
robotics, and more. When announced by PM Modi, the Alliance was envisioned as a 
$1 billion fund, but media reports indicate it has already reached $1.9 billion. Not 
bad, right? Some Alliance members are contributing capital, while others like NVIDIA 
will provide access to experts, training, and other resources. 
----------------Page (50) Break----------------
The Deep Tech Alliance was created to provide long-term, patient capital for 
deep-tech startups and projects. It will work with various financial partners—from 
DFIs to VC funds—to deploy capital in high-risk, high-return ventures. The initiative 
aims to place India on the global map of frontier tech development. It clearly 
demonstrates (1) the understanding that sovereignty and global leadership depend 
on a country's ability to master, develop, and deploy key non-commodity 
technologies and (2) India’s ability to build large-scale public-private 
partnerships—surely not a commodity on this planet. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
India is a major success in space, and what ISRO has achieved is truly impressive. 
But what many people overlook is that the country has also been innovating in 
institutions to accelerate the translation of technologies into market applications. A 
few years ago, it created NewSpace India. More recently, the country established the 
Anusandhan National Research Foundation (ANNRF), another innovative institution 
designed to bridge research and innovation. To leap into the future, it is essential to 
innovate in institutions, and India has been doing just that. Other countries should be 
watching and learning from its experience. Of course, like anywhere else, there is 
room for improvement and adjustment, but the direction is clear and sends a strong 
message. 
With its talent base, data scale, and geopolitical positioning, India has many of the 
elements needed to become the next pole of technological gravity. NVIDIA’s 
involvement—along with that of other corporations and venture firms—adds fuel to 
that vision, but it is not an isolated fact or a government-only effort. The country has 
an exceptional science and technology talent pool and, until recently, was mostly 
known for its digital services sector. Things may be changing. Recently, Google 
announced a massive $15 billion investment in an AI data hub in Bangalore. From 
2024 to 2025, investment in Indian deep tech grew by 78%. The global deep-tech 
race is accelerating, and India is positioning itself as a frontrunner.

 AI goes through the roof, to space! 
↳ Why it matters 
The demand for power used in data centers is soaring. In 2025 alone, it is projected 
to grow by 22%, and it is expected to triple by 2030. AI is hungry. Question: where will 
this power come from, and what are the impacts on Earth? Energy prices are rising, 
and Bloomberg reports that wholesale electricity costs have increased by as much 
as 267% in the last five years in areas near data centers. What is the solution to that? 
Well, MAG7 and other tech companies are betting on nuclear. But what if we had 
other solutions? 
Google Research just released a paper highlighting a fascinating concept: a 
space-based, scalable AI infrastructure. In plain terms, it’s about taking AI training 
and inference infrastructure off Earth—literally. The idea is to harness abundant 
energy availability in space—think of the Sun—and deploy space-based computing 
systems that can scale without the electricity limitations we face on Earth. Beyond 
energy, this points to the tremendous momentum that space, space tech, and the 
new space economy are gaining—and much more is to come. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
----------------Page (52) Break----------------
Satellites in sun-synchronous orbits can harvest up to eight times more solar energy 
than Earth-based panels. Instead of transmitting power back to Earth, Google 
proposes placing data centers directly in orbit. Far-fetched? Well, many companies 
are already developing solutions for that, such as Star Catcher (space power grid), 
Astrolight (space-to-space laser telecommunications), and Seldor Capital’s portfolio 
company LoneStar (data storage and processing). 
When we created Seldor Capital, we mapped various elements needed in space to 
advance exploration—food production, manufacturing, construction, power and 
telecom systems, data storage and processing, and more—and the types of 
solutions required to supply or enable those. In many cases, doing or making things 
in space could also offer advantages, such as lowering cooling and energy 
requirements for data centers. 
We tend to think of AI as software, but it is becoming infrastructure. Space offers 
what Earth increasingly lacks, such as abundant electricity. The question is no longer 
whether we can scale AI, but where we will do it. As we enter this new realm, we are 
building the foundations for interplanetary AI networks and moving from connecting 
devices to connecting assets in space and eventually celestial bodies. Paradoxically, 
the lines between space and Earth are also becoming increasingly blurred. 
↳ Read more here https://bit.ly/484C2Nk 
https://bit.ly/4ouvyNo 
https://bit.ly/3JKCVkS 
 The hidden thirst of Artificial  Intelligence 
↳ Why it matters 
----------------Page (53) Break----------------
A new Ecolab study released this past week reveals a startling fact: most consumers 
are unaware of AI’s massive water footprint. From cooling data centers to powering 
training clusters, the surge in generative AI is driving water use to unsustainable 
levels. While tech giants promise carbon neutrality, few talk about water neutrality, 
even though each ChatGPT query or AI-generated image can indirectly consume 
liters of water through energy and cooling cycles. In a world losing freshwater at an 
unprecedented rate, where 25% of the population lacks access to clean drinking 
water, this is a serious issue. But it also signals an opportunity for innovators and 
entrepreneurs—one that major tech companies are already beginning to notice. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
We marvel at the power of AI, but every digital miracle leaves a physical mark. The 
hidden infrastructure of AI—servers, cooling systems, uncountable miles of cables, 
and data hubs—drains water reservoirs worldwide. AI is not just hungry, it is thirsty. 
Ecolab’s research with people in 15 nations shows that only 41% of consumers 
recognize water use in AI operations, compared with 55% who acknowledge its 
significant   power consumption. There is still so much we don’t know. Did you know 
that? 
Some companies position themselves in the global market as clean data center 
providers and operators, using 100% renewable energy. MAG7 companies are 
making major clean energy purchases and betting on nuclear. But what is the next 
competitive edge for data center technology providers and operators? Water 
efficiency in AI. Perhaps the real test for AI is whether its intelligence can include 
self-awareness of its own resource impact and manage that. More to come on this 
front. Cheers!

 Are we ready for enhanced humans? 
↳ Why it matters 
France drew a line in the sand. Its sports authorities announced that any French 
athlete competing in the upcoming Enhanced Games—a new Olympic-style event 
that openly allows performance-enhancing drugs—will face sanctions. Set to debut 
----------------Page (55) Break----------------
in Las Vegas in May 2026, the Games promise $500,000 prizes per event and 
million-dollar bonuses for world-record performances. Supporters call it a bold 
experiment in human potential. Critics see it as the formalization of doping. For 
everyone else, it’s a clear signal that we’re entering uncharted territory—where 
technology could push human performance to new extremes and widen divides that 
may echo across generations. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
This isn’t just a sports story — it’s a cultural and existential one. The Enhanced 
Games are a mirror held up to our obsession with progress and limits. What happens 
when “human achievement” is no longer bound by biology, but by chemistry, 
technology, and capital? But... wait a minute — aren’t we already there? 
On one side, there’s the allure of freedom and innovation: athletes pushing the 
frontier of what bodies can do. On the other, a moral and medical gray zone that 
could redefine   fairness, health, victory, and what being human really means. Is this a 
stretch? Not really. The race to select and engineer ‘better humans’ is already 
underway — and accelerating. We might be witnessing the moment when sport, like 
so many other human arenas, begins to split: between the natural and the 
engineered. The haves and the have-nots. The ares and the are-nots. Are we ready 
for that?

 The Argentinean experiment gets a boost 
↳ Why it matters 
----------------Page (56) Break----------------
Javier Milei’s party, La Libertad Avanza, scored a sweeping victory in Argentina’s 
midterm elections, winning around 40 percent of the vote and dramatically 
expanding its presence in Congress. In the lower chamber, it jumped from 37 to 80 
seats, while in the Senate, its representation tripled to 20 seats. The result 
strengthens Milei’s mandate to advance his vision — slashing public spending, 
cutting taxes, deregulating the economy and redefining  the role of the state. 
Beyond Argentina’s borders, the outcome reverberates across Latin America and the 
world. It can be seen as part of a broader ideological realignment in the region — but 
also as a bold experiment in overhauling an entire economy and society. Will it 
succeed? Time — and the people — will tell. The real question is whether Milei can 
sustain popular support for his reformist push while loosening labor regulations and 
challenging entrenched interests. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
Argentina is the only country in the world that went from being one of the richest to 
becoming a developing one. In 1910, its GDP per capita was 80% of the U.S. level; 
today, it’s around 15%. When did it go south? My take: it started long ago, in the late 
1930s. After the Great Depression, two key things happened. First, working-class 
demands were not met with real solutions. Discontent grew, paving the way for a 
state-controlled labor union system. Second, the country stuck to its agro-export 
model — elites failed to reinvest the capital they had accumulated into building a 
diversified,  future-oriented economy. Which country did the opposite? The United 
States under FDR. The New Deal expanded industrial capacity and innovation, and 
WWII supercharged it, turning the U.S. into the innovation powerhouse we know 
today. 
Back to Argentina: what’s happening under the Milei administration is an experiment 
— a bold attempt to rewrite the rules of the economy and society (according to 
Milei's view, naturally), hoping to cure decades of inflation, stagnation, erosion of 
capabilities and mistrust. Facts are still unfolding and results remain to be seen. 
----------------Page (57) Break----------------
Argentina’s journey from the 1940s to the 2020s has been turbulent — marked by 
economic instability, dictatorship, a stupid war and decline. In the early 1970s, 
poverty was around 5%; today, it’s over 35%, down from 55% at the height of Milei’s 
initial reforms in 2024. For decades, poverty was addressed through a patchwork of 
social programs and government initiatives, not by tackling the structural causes of 
underperformance. Fixing that is tough — far easier said and written from my couch 
than done. 
Milei’s 2023 election was more than a political victory — it was a declaration that 
enough is enough. People, exhausted by decades of mismanagement, wanted a 
solution, whatever it might be. Milei turned rage into a political engine and 
economics into identity. Whether Argentina finds prosperity or deeper fracture will 
hinge on one question: can the dismantling of social programs and government 
interventions coexist with social stability? The midterm election results send a clear 
signal: despite the pain, Argentines are choosing to go further down Milei’s path. 
Whatever happens next, Argentina has become a must-follow case for the world.

 We need leaders equipped to avoid false climate 
paradoxes 
↳ Why it matters 
Bill Gates made headlines last week with a clear message: we need to reprioritize 
climate investments. Around the same time, Brazil approved a license for Petrobras 
to drill an offshore oil well—500 km (311 miles) from the mouth of the Amazon 
River. All of this unfolds as the country prepares to host COP30. To many, these 
----------------Page (58) Break----------------
moves look contradictory—even counter to the fight against climate change. But 
that may not be the case. They reflect the complex balancing act of our time: 
securing energy, lifting people out of poverty (and strengthening social contracts in 
the process), advancing climate goals and adapting to a world already being 
reshaped by climate change. 
Extreme weather events are no longer rare. As we speak, another hurricane has 
devastated Jamaica and parts of the Caribbean. And while the impacts of climate 
change are global, so is the need for investment. Climate change is real—it’s 
happening—and fighting it demands new technology, capital and economic models. 
We’re seeing progress. In the first half of 2025, global power demand grew by 369 
TWh, but solar and wind additions exceeded that growth by 9%. For the first time, 
we’re adding more clean power than demand is rising. That’s remarkable. Yet, 
technology alone won’t solve the problem if we can’t fund environmental law 
enforcement, innovation and inclusion—especially in places like the Amazon, where 
people will resort to low-productivity, high-impact activities, like cutting down 
forests, if they lack alternatives and support to make a living. Paradoxically, 
developing fossil energy resources can generate the capital needed to fund those 
transitions and protections. Leaders must be able to weigh all these 
dimensions—and we should vote for those who can. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
In Brazil, 47% of emissions come from land-use change, agriculture, and 
deforestation—a very different profile  from global emissions. And behind those 
emissions are 40 million people living in the Amazon region, many without access 
to quality jobs, education, capital, or opportunities to thrive. If we fail to provide 
economic alternatives, they will continue turning to low-productivity activities like 
cutting trees just to survive. That’s the point. Sustainability is not only about 
protecting the forest—it’s about people, productivity and prosperity. It’s about 
----------------Page (59) Break----------------
ensuring that transitions are economically viable, that innovation reaches the 
ground and that people and places are not left behind. 
At the GFCC, the message we keep reinforcing is clear: sustainability happens at 
the intersection of economy, society, and the environment. We can’t treat those 
dimensions as separate. We need to mobilize investment to build capabilities, scale 
new technologies and lift communities. Yes, it’s complex—but there is no other way 
forward. As we move toward COP30 in Brazil, it’s time to look beyond the headlines 
and focus on the bigger picture. Real climate leadership will come not from 
choosing between energy and environment, but from connecting the dots, aligning 
incentives and building systems that work for people on the ground.

 Are We One Outage Away from Digital Chaos? 
↳ Why it matters 
On October 20, Amazon Web Services experienced a major outage that disrupted 
thousands of websites and apps worldwide, from banks and streaming platforms to 
government services and smart homes. The issue originated in a malfunction in its 
DNS automation system in the US-EAST-1 region and quickly cascaded across the 
network. This exposed a deeper vulnerability: a large portion of the global internet 
now runs on the infrastructure of just a few cloud giants. When one stumbles, the 
ripple effects are global. 
This isn’t just a technical glitch, it’s a structural issue: we are putting way too many 
eggs in one really few baskets. Our digital economy has become highly concentrated 
in the hands of a few providers, creating dependencies that extend far beyond 
convenience. As AI systems, payment networks, healthcare services, and even 
national security increasingly rely on cloud infrastructure, the resilience of our 
societies now hinges on a handful of data centers owned by a few firms. 
----------------Page (61) Break----------------
↳ My thoughts on it 
The internet was once seen as a great equalizer, a universal network meant to 
spread information, foster connections and open space for global dialogue. It 
promised more openness, more inclusion and more democracy. The cloud brought a 
new promise: flexibility, scale and speed. But today, infrastructure equals power. The 
cloud is no longer a neutral layer, it has become a geopolitical, economic, and 
societal lever. And power has become concentrated. AWS holds 30% of the global 
cloud market, ahead of Microsoft’s 20% and Google’s 12%. In a world where AI feeds 
on data, those who own the servers and control the flows have the upper hand. 
Companies, startups, and even governments are no longer just customers, they are 
now subjects in a new era of digital sovereignty, for which rules are being written in 
real time. And while major powers try to shape the rules, one big question remains: 
Can smaller or developing countries really set any terms, or will they just have to 
adapt? What’s at stake isn’t just operational resilience. It’s control, agency and the 
architecture of our digital future. 
↳ Read more here 
● https://tinyurl.com/mvntm9a8 
● https://tinyurl.com/53383acz 
 The Next Digital Frontier Is Biological 
↳ Why it matters 
This past week, multiple labs reported breakthroughs in using AI to model cellular 
behavior. At the same time, the U.S. Cybersecurity and Infrastructure Security Agency

----------------Page (62) Break----------------
biotechnology will be a the center of that, with new investments in bioinformatics 
and genome engineering being announced. Digital biotechnology becomes 
increasingly important in today's global competition, its development raises fresh 
questions about data sovereignty and biological IP protection. As biology becomes 
code, the infrastructure of life itself enters the domain of cybersecurity. Leaks or 
manipulations here could have consequences far beyond a data breach. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
For years, we treated biology as physical and digital as virtual. That line is dissolving 
fast. As genomics, synthetic biology, and AI converge, our ability to read, write, and 
simulate life is accelerating, and so are the risks. Cybersecurity is no longer just 
about data. It’s about DNA. It's cyberbiosecurity. The same AI that powers life-saving 
therapies can be used to design pathogens, alter genomes, or exfiltrate genetic data. 
This “bio-knowledge explosion” brings a twin: a bio-security challenge. If recent cloud 
failures have taught us anything, it’s that interconnected systems are only as strong 
as their weakest layer. But what happens when that layer is the foundation of life 
itself? We’ve reached a new frontier, where cybersecurity meets bioengineering, and 
where defending digital systems also means defending our biological future.

↳ Why it matters

----------------Page (63) Break----------------

globally. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
Europe has long been the most vocal advocate for global climate policy, but

diplomatic trade-offs, but also creates major opportunities for innovation. Like other 
technological shifts, decarbonization will produce big winners, those who bring new

a global standard-setter. And let’s be honest: how could European leaders expect to 
poke Russia with NATO expansion, underinvest in defense, still wait for the US to pay 
the bill and come to rescue them and keep energy prices stable thanks to the gas 
supply from Russia? The question now is not whether Europe leads, but whether it 
can reinvent its model of leadership... before others fill the vacuum.

 The Death of Truth: When Seeing Is No Longer 
Believing 
↳ Why it matters 
We’ve entered an era where truth itself is under siege. OpenAI’s Sora can now 
generate ultra-realistic videos from simple text prompts, an astonishing leap forward 
in AI’s creative capability. But with this progress comes an uncomfortable reality: 
fake news is no longer crude or detectable. As tools like Sora democratize the power 
to create lifelike video fakes, uncertainty and risk are multiplying across every layer 
of society. Trust, already fragile, is eroding even further. The old saying “seeing is 
believing” no longer holds true; today, what the eyes see may deceive the heart. From 
----------------Page (65) Break----------------
cybersecurity and crime to manipulation of public debate, the implications are 
immense. This shift will not only redefine   how we verify truth, but also introduce new 
costs and risks across cybersecurity, commerce, and human relationships. We’re not 
just witnessing a technological advance—we’re facing the birth of a new information 
battlefield.   
↳ My thoughts on it 
It’s both fascinating and deeply concerning. What we’re seeing with Sora is not a 
glitch, it’s the next frontier. Soon, it will be almost impossible to tell what’s real and 
what’s not. There are already cases—like a 2024 incident where a fund manager 
wired $25 million after a deepfake Zoom call with someone who looked and sounded 
exactly like his boss. These are not science fiction anymore. They’re happening now. 
So, what comes next? We’ll need new layers of verification, biometric, behavioral, 
maybe even genetic. The future of trust will depend on authentication. Just as your 
browser shows a security lock, our calls, videos, and even human interactions may 
soon display a “trust score.” In the end, AI isn’t killing truth, it’s forcing us to reinvent 
it.

----------------Page (67) Break----------------

their complexity. Look at today’s weight-loss injections, remarkably effective, but with 
still-unclear neurological effects. The more we digitize biology, the more we’ll

lowers costs and truly expands access to better health, not just accelerates the price

 A Next Billion-Dollar Layer of the Sky 
↳ Why it matters 
A week ago, dozens of drones suddenly fell from the sky in China—check the footage 
in the links. The incident highlights the rise of a new economy quietly forming just 
above our heads—between ground level and a few thousand feet in the air—where 
China is the dominant player. It also shows how far we still have to go before this 
“low-altitude economy” can safely take off, especially when it comes to securing 
drone operations (and protecting everyone below). 
The potential of this new economy is enormous, but for now, it remains highly 
concentrated. In Hong Kong alone, 38 pilot projects are already testing low-altitude 
operations. One Chinese company—DJI—controls about 70% of the global drone 
market. Across China, more than 14,000 companies are authorized to operate 
drones, with 225,000 licensed pilots and thousands more in training to power this 
booming aerial sector. 
Governments worldwide are racing to catch up and define  the rules that will enable 
this new economic layer. In the U.S., the FAA is considering removing the line-of-sight 
requirement for drone operations. But safety, regulation, communication networks, 
air traffic  systems and cyber-resilience all need to mature quickly. The sky may well 
be the next economic frontier—but only if we can make it safe. 
----------------Page (69) Break----------------
↳ My thoughts on it 
This emerging economy spans cargo delivery between ports and cities, drone light 
shows and electric vertical take-off and landing vehicles (eVTOLs) transporting 
people in dense urban areas. It’s where aviation, urban mobility, logistics, security 
and digital infrastructure converge. But managing thousands of autonomous flying 
machines in crowded urban environments demands real-time communication, 
AI-driven air traffic  control, reliable energy systems and, above all, trust and 
functional institutions — it’s a massive systems challenge. 
Solving it will require coordination among governments, technology developers, 
infrastructure and logistics operators and the public — one misstep away from chaos 
in the skies. It will take heavy investment and constant innovation. Can other 
countries catch up and challenge China in the years ahead? Europe has launched a 
drone strategy — will it translate into a real industrial push? In the U.S., the 
administration aims to accelerate unmanned aerial vehicle integration into business 
operations through more flexible regulation. The first countries to master this and 
build the industrial capacity to scale solutions won’t just create a new industry — 
they’ll own the new sky. 
↳ Read more here

 Humanoids are Coming to Your Home 
↳ Why it matters 
Two days ago, robotics startup Figure AI dropped a major signal: its next-generation 
humanoid, Figure 03, is moving out of the lab and toward real-world deployment. 
----------------Page (70) Break----------------
This isn’t a minor upgrade from Figure 02—it’s a bold bet that robots are ready to 
leave factories and enter households, logistics hubs and everyday environments. 
Figure’s announcement marks a major inflection point for robotics: the shift from 
robots for industry to robots for life. If they succeed in building a capable, safe and 
affordable robot that works seamlessly around humans, it could spark a wave of 
adoption that reshapes labor markets, home design and regulation. For now, the 
projected price tag is between $20,000 and $30,000—roughly the cost of an 
entry-level car in the U.S.—about what Elon Musk has floated for Tesla’s Optimus. 
Meanwhile, in China, Engine AI is promising a humanoid companion priced around 
$5,300. The age of humanoid robots is here to stay. But we’re still in the early 
days—and both the market and the industry are just beginning to stand up. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
As in most critical technology arenas, the U.S. and China are locked in fierce 
competition. Dozens of companies across both countries—and a few in Europe—are 
racing to develop humanoid robots. Curiously, Japan, which once pioneered robotics, 
now seems to be lagging behind, with its efforts still focused on industrial robots or 
entertainment-style machines that look more like toys or cartoon characters than 
humanoids. 
Back to Figure AI—and to what may give them an edge. Their Figure 02 model has 
reportedly been operating autonomously at a BMW manufacturing site in Germany 
for months. The company is building its own battery system, designing for mass 
production, and has already demonstrated early walking and manipulation 
milestones. By choosing to own the full technology stack—battery, actuators, 
software, and hardware—Figure gains tighter control over integration and reliability. 
Their partnership with Brookfield,  one of the world’s largest real estate developers, to 
create large-scale datasets and infrastructure hints at a broader vision: robots not 
just as prototypes, but as deployable assets at global scale. Even if Figure 03 doesn’t 
yet cook dinner or fold laundry next year, its launch pushes the entire field 
----------------Page (71) Break----------------
forward—especially in the West. It raises the bar for competitors, regulators, and 
suppliers alike. 
And speaking of competitors—have you seen the latest Tesla Optimus videos? In one 
clip, the robot performs a karate kata, even sparring briefly  with a human. Impressive, 
yes—but also unsettling. Imagine what happens when these machines get really 
good. What if one goes rogue? The future of humanoids is coming fast—and it’s 
equal parts exciting and terrifying. Are we ready for that?

 Computing Beyond the Cloud 
↳ Why it matters 
The computer cloud may soon leave Earth’s atmosphere. A few days ago in Italy, Jeff 
Bezos predicted that within 10 to 20 years, we could see data centers in orbit, 
powered by limitless solar energy and cooled by the vacuum of space. From 
Starcloud’s and Lonestar’s orbital and lunar prototypes to Axiom Space’s modular 
computing stations, a new frontier is emerging—where aerospace meets digital 
infrastructure. To advance space exploration, as we mapped at Seldor Capital, we’ll 
need computing and storage capacity in space—not to mention capabilities to 
produce food, fuel and manufactured goods beyond Earth. What seemed futuristic a 
few years ago, when we invested in LoneStar, is now entering the mainstream. We 
bet on that. 
----------------Page (72) Break----------------
There are also very earthly reasons for this shift. Running AI and data processing in 
orbit could mean unlimited clean energy, near-zero cooling costs, and global 
redundancy against terrestrial failures. But new opportunities bring new dilemmas: 
who regulates data outside national borders? How do you patch a server 500 
kilometers above the ground? While the technical challenges are immense, the 
governance and regulatory questions may prove even harder. Space-based data 
centers will force humanity to rewrite the rules for how we store, protect and access 
Earth-originated information—ushering in not just a new computing era, but a new 
chapter in digital sovereignty. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
We tend to think of “the cloud” as intangible, but in reality, it’s built on billions of 
watts of power, tons of steel, and massive cooling systems. And data centers keep 
multiplying. Moving that infrastructure into orbit is the ultimate abstraction — digital 
infrastructure literally detached from the planet. While this may sound like science 
fiction — and yes, I was once asked if I was crazy for investing in it — there’s a solid 
economic rationale behind the idea, beyond the needs of space missions 
themselves. The CAPEX may be enormous, but the OPEX of orbital data centers 
could be significantly  lower than on Earth. Why? Because cooling and energy — two 
of the biggest operational costs — are virtually free in space, thanks to the vacuum 
and constant solar exposure. 
Whoever builds the first functional, secure space data center will become a pioneer 
in interplanetary computing. Still, reliability and repair remain huge hurdles — you 
can’t just send a technician on a rocket for a reboot. But that challenge will, in turn, 
create new opportunities for space robotics and autonomous maintenance systems. 
The future of computing won’t just be distributed — it will be distributed at a 
planetary, and soon interplanetary, scale. Fasten your seatbelts. We’re accelerating 
toward that future, even if most people still can’t quite visualize it.

Weekly Snapshot - Oct 04, 2025 
 
Another week, another Weekly Snapshot. And this one is packed. From AI that 
doesn’t just advise but actually sells you stuff, to science that may soon help us 
regenerate organs, and even bold ideas of turning the Amazon into an engine of 
innovation. Big shifts are happening at the intersection of tech, health, and nature. 
Ready to dive in? 
 Is ChatGPT turning into an AI-powered storefront? 
↳ Why It Matters 
OpenAI just launched Instant Checkout: U.S. users can now buy items from Etsy 
directly inside ChatGPT, with Shopify integration coming soon. No 
----------------Page (74) Break----------------
redirects—purchases happen natively. Merchants pay commissions; users pay 
nothing extra. This marks a shift from AI as adviser to AI as actor—the platform 
doesn’t just suggest, it executes. OpenAI is moving beyond subscriptions into 
commerce, challenging e-commerce infrastructure incumbents. With the global 
e-commerce market at ~$30 trillion (B2B included) and digital ads at $600–850 
billion, the potential is massive. 
↳ My Thoughts On It 
This is bold—embedding commerce directly into conversation compresses 
discovery, decision and purchase. The potential for AI-powered e-commerce is huge, 
and we’re just scratching the surface. If OpenAI nails trust, fairness in rankings and 
fraud defense, this could rewrite how we shop online. The ripple effects on digital 
ads and e-commerce could be massive. Markets are watching: between Sep 29 and 
Oct 1, Google shares dropped 1.7% before recovering. Amazon? Its stock actually 
rose on the 29th. But this is just the beginning. 
OpenAI generated $4.3B in revenue in the first half of 2025—already 16% more than 
all of 2024. Still, it burned $2.5B in the same period. AI is changing everything, but AI 
companies need new revenue streams. As they build them, others will lose slices of 
the pie.

 Can we regenerate organs? The regenerative 
frontier accelerates 
↳ Why It Matters 
----------------Page (75) Break----------------
A new study published in September 2025 shows that stem cell injections can 
reverse brain damage from stroke in mice—the cells survive, differentiate and 
connect with native neurons over weeks. Earlier this year, Stanford scientists also 
reported success growing vascularized mini-organs (heart, liver organoids) with their 
own blood vessels—a major step toward lab-grown organs for therapies. 
A whole new wave of therapies is likely to reach the market in the coming decade. 
We may finally be moving from damage control to regeneration and, later, to 
prevention at the molecular and cellular level. New research and commercial 
application areas will emerge, creating value and reshaping health systems, 
economies and societies. Those developing and bringing new health technologies to 
market will generate major returns, and people around the world will benefit,   
although access will remain a challenge, especially at the start. 
↳My Thoughts On It 
We’re inching closer to a future where organ damage isn’t permanent. The leap from 
small animal models and organoids to human-scale safe therapies is still huge, but 
the pace is accelerating. The real test will be translation: scaling, regulatory safety, 
immune compatibility. Yet the direction is transformative. 
In 1900, the average life expectancy at birth was 32 years. Today, the global average 
is more than 73. Most of that progress came from public health, vaccines and 
antibiotics. We succeeded in dealing with many exogenous causes of disease, but 
our bodies still degenerate over time. Non-infectious chronic diseases remain the 
main causes of death and disability in advanced and even developing economies. 
Why do bodies degenerate? In short, because the balance between damage and 
self-repair tips toward damage. At some point in the future—hopefully sooner rather 
than later—we may be able to move beyond restoring function to actually preventing 
cellular and molecular degeneration. 
But don’t forget: in any scenario, it’s still your task to take care of your own body. 
----------------Page (76) Break----------------
↳ Read more here 
● https://tinyurl.com/5n6msc3f 
● https://tinyurl.com/hfxk7j6b 
● https://tinyurl.com/5d88ahav 
● https://tinyurl.com/4dmwsuu9 
 Can the Amazon become an innovation engine? 
↳ Why It Matters 
Brazil was on the spot during New York’s Climate Week 2025, and the potential of the 
Brazilian bioeconomy was highlighted last week by the Swiss State Secretary for 
Education, Research and Innovation—far away from the Amazon, right? 
A key talking point: Brazil’s bioeconomy is projected to generate hundreds of billions 
of US dollars annually by leveraging the Amazon’s biological diversity (pharma, 
biomaterials, food). The environmental services the Amazon as a whole—not just the 
Brazilian part—provides are valued at $275 billion per year, according to NIH 
estimates. Water regulation alone accounts for about 60% of the income of Brazilian 
agribusiness. And the impacts go beyond Brazil—the U.S. Midwest is “irrigated” by 
water pumped into the atmosphere by the Amazon. 
If Brazil and other Amazonian countries could better understand—we know so 
little—and create value from the forest, that would be a net positive for them and for 
the world. New models of socio-bioeconomy are emerging, combining Indigenous 
knowledge, conservation and high-tech, but they are still nascent and don't have 
scale. Much more is needed through a consistent and steady commitment to 
building a knowledge-intensive Amazonian economy. 
↳ My Thoughts On It 
----------------Page (77) Break----------------
These days, the focus on Brazil has clear reasons: it is the most biodiverse country in 
the world (and—what most people don’t know—it also has the most diverse human 
population, check the links), hosts about 60% of the Amazon forest and will host 
COP30. But how can we create more value from the Amazon? Preservation is 
essential, but the real bottleneck to building a robust bioeconomy is not nature—it’s 
research, business capabilities, structures and resources. Nature alone is not 
enough. 
A fundamental part of the equation must be understanding nature. We lack data, 
information and even tools. Initiatives such as the XPrize Rainforest are critical in 
this context. The need for nature tech entrepreneurs and companies is gigantic. 
Global cooperation could help, but through new models. Instead of traditional ODA, 
imagine top global universities establishing campuses and operations in the 
Amazon. The Swiss seem to have an eye on that. Who else? 
↳ Read more here 
● https://tinyurl.com/y6fbmzfu 
● https://tinyurl.com/2u8mjpan 
● https://tinyurl.com/2jtea2dx

Weekly Snapshot - Sep 28, 2025 
 
 Are we witnessing the birth of a new business 
model for space? 
↳ Why it matters 
Vast Space, a super well-funded US startup, made headlines last week all the way to 
New Zealand—a country betting on space tech as a driver of future growth. The 
company has reached a key milestone in building Haven-1, the world’s first 
commercial space station, planned for launch into low-Earth orbit by May 2026. 
Haven-1 is now fully welded—watch the video on Vast’s website. Designed with 
human comfort in mind, it features private sleeping quarters, panoramic windows, 
Starlink internet and optimized workspaces. 
Unlike the International Space Station, always a government-led initiative, Haven-1 
will be privately owned and operated. It is conceived as a platform for scientific   
research, in-orbit manufacturing and private astronaut missions, even opening the 
door to space tourism. But the real breakthrough is its concept: “space-as-a-service.” 
Agencies, companies and individuals will be able to rent orbital time and 
infrastructure, and even share the investment. A new business and organizational 
model will be put to the test. If Vast succeeds, 2026 may be remembered as the year 
space tech adopted a new business model. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
The rise of private spacetech mirrors, in some ways, what happened in the early 
years of commercial aviation, when state-led development slowly gave way to a 
vast—pun intended—industry. 
----------------Page (79) Break----------------
Haven-1 still has a way to go, and if it actually launches and operates successfully in 
2026, Haven-2—potentially replacing the ISS—would only come in 2032. Vast’s 
aggressive outreach to partners signals that space is becoming a domain where 
CAPEX can be shared, and infrastructure itself becomes an asset to be rented. Think 
efficiency in the use of space assets. From my auto industry background, this feels 
like a mix of Volkswagen’s “modular consortium” in its Brazilian truck plant and a 
shared infrastructure you pay per use. 
A personal note: Interstellar Lab, a company Seldor Capital has invested in, is one of 
Vast’s partners. Its plant growth chamber, Eden 1.0, will be integrated into the lab. 
Exciting times are ahead, and I’ll be watching closely.

 Can we predict the sun before it knocks out our 
systems? 
↳ Why it matters 
Last Wednesday, NASA and NOAA launched three new missions—IMAP, the 
Carruthers Geocorona Observatory and SWFO-L1—to map how the Sun interacts with 
the Solar System and monitor solar storms that threaten satellites, power grids and 
even internet cables, datacenters and microprocessors on Earth. These launches 
highlight how protecting space and electronic infrastructures has become a critical 
----------------Page (80) Break----------------
priority as our dependence on satellites and computation deepens across all 
technological layers. You may not notice it, but much of what you do relies on space 
technologies—and when solar storms hit, the costs are felt on Earth. Just imagine if 
GPS stopped working. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
As orbits grow more crowded and reliance on space, power and data infrastructures 
deepens, predicting solar storms with greater accuracy will be essential to mitigate 
failures, damage and disruptions across critical chains like communications, 
networks and navigation. This new generation of space-weather sensors could 
become part of the backbone of resilience—in space and on Earth. 
The bigger issue is how vulnerable we are to disruptions in power and computation, 
things most of the world takes for granted and rarely sees. Risk management 
remains poorly established and even less valued than it should be. The question is: 
what risks are we not aware of and not managing in our complex planet?

 What does China’s new emissions pledge say to the 
world? 
↳ Why it matters 
----------------Page (81) Break----------------
China is the world’s largest carbon emitter—what it does, or doesn’t do, matters for 
everyone. This past week, in a video statement during the UN General Assembly, 
President Xi Jinping pledged to peak emissions by 2030 and cut them by 7–10% by 
2035. The scientific   consensus is clear: this is not enough to keep global warming 
below 1.5°C—or even 2.0°C. Europe quickly responded, calling China’s goals 
insufficient.  China, in turn, accused the EU of hypocrisy. But this goes far beyond 
diplomatic sparring. The stakes are geopolitical. With the U.S. stepping back from 
international climate leadership, a void is emerging. And in climate—as in 
geopolitics—there are no vacuums. Someone will fill the space. From EVs to solar, 
batteries to grid-scale renewables, China has invested heavily and leads in multiple 
clean tech markets—and ‘climate leadership’ has special significance  and value in 
that backdrop. But the math is brutal: if the world’s biggest polluter doesn’t do 
more—and all other too, we all fall short. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
Yes, China has made remarkable progress in clean technology. It leads the world in 
solar panels, battery production and electric vehicles. But momentum matters in 
building a global market presence. So does perception. China’s long-term industrial 
bets are inseparable from its climate policy and geopolitical standing. Still, let’s be 
honest. The global climate shortfall isn’t China’s fault alone. Most advanced and 
emerging economies are also falling behind. What makes this moment troubling is 
the widening gap between what’s needed and what’s being done—by everyone. If this 
is the defining  challenge of our time, we must ask: are countries truly preparing for 
the world that’s coming? The window is closing.

 Why is Mexico raising tariffs on Chinese EVs? 
↳ Why it matters  
Mexico’s decision to impose tariffs on Chinese automotive imports is portrayed in 
the media as having sparked outrage in Beijing. But as we all know, this is not just 
about cars. Deeply integrated with the U.S. economy, the country is dancing a 
complex dance with its neighbor in the north while trying to keep its auto 
manufacturing sector afloat. So far, the dance with the U.S. seems to be working and 
underscores Claudia Sheinbaum’s administration’s ability to navigate turbulent 
waters—others should learn from that. Strictly on cars, Mexico’s move—coupled with 
new restrictions in Europe—challenges China’s expanding global footprint in electric 
vehicles and green manufacturing. Things may not be so easy after all. It calls for a 
----------------Page (83) Break----------------
strategy review in China’s auto industry and in government policies backing its global 
expansion. Still, it is too early—only time will tell what really happens. 
↳ My thoughts on it  
By hitting Chinese vehicles, Mexico is sending signals to Washington, Beijing and 
also to Puebla, Guanajuato and DF–think Mexican automakers. The country is 
betting on its strategic proximity to the U.S. and the future of that relationship—under 
Trump and beyond. Think about this: Mexico has a strategy, and the U.S. is critical to 
it. I first heard this clearly from one of Mexico’s top economists during a visit two 
years ago. But here’s the catch—the strategy is not deliberate, it emerged over time, 
matured and is now consolidated across the political spectrum. Geography matters. 
In doing so, Mexico stands in stark contrast to other emerging nations that 
completely lack strategy. 
This episode also highlights a broader truth: the energy transition—EVs, batteries, 
supply chains—is as much geopolitical as it is technological. The future of cars is 
about trade and strategic alliances, not just engineering.

Beyond the gadget appeal, this is Meta’s bigger bet: AI + AR as the foundation of the

and daily life. The real implication? Once one of these devices breaks through in the 
market, we’ll hit a turning point—after which the very meaning of phone, computer 
and interface will be rewritten. 
↳ My thoughts on it

potentially giving ourselves a cognitive co-pilot. The big question is: will people 
embrace this intimacy with AI, or reject it as invasive? 
With Google Glass, the stated issue was privacy, but I believe the real problem was 
performance. From weak displays outdoors to short battery life and the enormous 
challenge of embedding real processing power into AR devices, the limitations of AR 
glasses that have reached the market so far are clear. Are we now reaching the point 
where performance will finally meet the minimum bar customers expect?

have humans at the bench in a few years? The first fully AI-designed drug isn’t here 
yet, but it’s coming. And when it does, drug pipelines, pharma business models and 
regulatory systems will all need to adapt.

nowhere near today’s level. Now the time has come, and you don’t even need 
supercomputers. This is not only about faster timelines; it’s about who will own the 
“knowledge infrastructure” of life. If private tech players like DeepMind become the 
essential engines of biomedical discovery, how will governments, pharma 
companies, regulators and public health institutions keep up? The upside for 
Google’s DeepMind and other AI powerhouses is gigantic. “AI as a service” for big

Weekly Snapshot - Sept 14, 2025 
 
From AI cabinet ministers to billion-dollar biotech bets and Europe’s boldest play yet 
in the AI race, the boundaries of technology, governance, and health are being 
----------------Page (87) Break----------------
redrawn right in front of us. What once felt like distant scenarios, machines shaping 
policy, pharma giants chasing precision cures, Europe flexing tech sovereignty, are 
now hard realities knocking on our doors. 
Let’s dive in! 
 We are witnessing the inauguration of the world’s 
first AI-cabinet minister 
↳ Why it matters 
Albania, under Prime Minister Edi Rama, has appointed Diella, an AI-powered virtual 
assistant, as a legitimate ‘cabinet minister’—really?—in charge of public procurement 
to fight systemic corruption. This is not merely symbolic: launched in January 2025 
on Albania’s e-Albania digital services portal to assist citizens with state paperwork, 
Diella will now oversee the awarding of government tenders. Rama has pledged that 
with Diella, Albania will achieve 100% corruption-free public tenders. This marks a 
new step in using AI in government administration. It will be a test—for the 
technology, for the government and for the idea that AI-based automation can 
reduce corruption. It also raises urgent questions about accountability, oversight, 
bias and how “virtual” actors perform in traditionally human-dominated roles. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
Are we entering a moment where “virtual ministers” are no longer science fiction but 
real policy tools? On one hand, using AI in governance to reduce bias, bribery or 
favoritism in procurement could be transformative. On the other, handing such a 
critical function to software gives enormous power to those who design and train it. 
AI already outperforms humans in some areas. It can better assess bail decisions, as 
Malcolm Gladwell notes in Talking to Strangers. It can map connections among laws 
at a scale no human can. In the US, about 200,000 bills are introduced in state 
----------------Page (88) Break----------------
houses each year. I recall meeting FiscalNote’s founders in Silicon Valley in 
2013—they pioneered the use of AI at scale to make sense of legislation and the 
implications across the legal system. In 2014, a top Brazilian lawyer friend told me: 
“As of today, 95% of the work lawyers do in Brazil can already be automated with AI.” 
Imagine today. Again in Brazil, the Federal administration is using AI to process 
social security claims and disputes. But procurement is different. It involves complex 
technical and social processes, often in multiple phases. Corruption often stems 
from inside information or revolving doors. 
So can an AI system be manipulated or gamed? What does “transparent” mean when 
decisions are algorithmic and opaque? How will citizens ensure Diella is fair? And 
how will the EU and others react, given Albania’s ambition to join the bloc by 2030?

 Europe’s AI sovereignty play: ASML  buys into Mistral 
↳ Why it matters

deal highlights a coordinated push to build European AI sovereignty in the face of US 
and Chinese dominance. As noted before, AI is not just about technology—it is about 
geopolitics, industrial strength and control over critical infrastructure. This move 
signals Europe’s intent to close the investment gap and retain talent, aiming to be 
more than a regulatory power in tech. Still, compared with the US and China, it 
remains a drop in the ocean. 
----------------Page (89) Break----------------
↳ My thoughts on it 
For years, Europe has been better known for writing AI rules than for producing AI 
giants. The ASML-Mistral deal points to a bolder vision: pairing Europe’s crown jewel 
in semiconductor manufacturing gear with its most promising AI startup. Is that 
really the case? To be seen. But the three Mistral co-founders have already become 
the first AI billionaires in France. Mistral raised $2 billion in total, with $500 million 
coming from seven other funds and Nvidia—only one of them French. The question 
is whether Europe can combine capital at scale with the dynamism and vision 
needed to compete with the firepower of US and Chinese ecosystems. For the 
skeptics, note that just last week tech funding deals in Europe topped €3.1 billion. 
The old world may still deliver new surprises. The talent is there, what’s missing?

Weekly Snapshot - Sept 6, 2025 
 
We live in a moment where boundaries blur, science gets politicized, and even the 
climate becomes a stage for venture bets. Some stories feel like glimpses of 
tomorrow breaking into today. Others sound like fiction until they aren’t. 
What happens when shopping skips the search bar, when satellites go dark, or when 
investors start reaching for the sun itself? 
Let’s dive in. 
 The boundaries between the physical and digital are 
blurring, and AR will only accelerate the shift 
↳ Why it matters 
Amazon has just launched Lens Live, an AI-powered tool that lets people point their 
phone at an object in the real world—say, a pair of sneakers on the street—and 
instantly get buying options. It’s the company’s latest attempt to bridge physical and 
digital shopping, and to make the world itself one big catalog. The move puts 
Amazon into a growing race, where Google (with Lens), Snap, and TikTok are also 
experimenting with computer vision shopping. The difference: Amazon controls the 
largest marketplace, logistics network, and payments backbone. If it succeeds in 
making real-world product recognition seamless, the “search bar” could shift from 
text to camera. That’s not just a UX update; it’s a new front in the war for how we 
discover and buy, but also an important clue on the future of interfaces and digital 
experiences. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
----------------Page (92) Break----------------
There’s something fascinating here: for years, e-commerce lived in a parallel world of 
clicks and recommendations, while physical life had its own messy frictions—stores, 
brands, inventory, discovery. Lens Live hints at the collapse of that boundary. 
Imagine walking through a city where every product is instantly “clickable.” Part of 
me sees convenience, part of me sees overload. What’s your take? 
But this is just the beginning. As augmented reality glasses reach the market, the 
very concept of the mobile phone will change—dissolving into new devices (glasses, 
wristbands and more) and unleashing an explosion of applications. Dozens of AR 
glasses projects are expected to reach the market in 2026–2027. The future will tell 
whether what we all expect—an indistinguishable object from ordinary glasses, but 
connected, AI-powered, with real processing power and convincing AR 
capabilities—will be part of that pack or not. Still, the path is clear. What Amazon is 
releasing is a crack through which we can glimpse the future. (By the way... have you 
seen Snapchat’s glasses?)

 Yes, we need to look down, up, left, right, back and 
beyond 
↳ Why it matters 
It’s no secret that a major shift is underway in the US and globally. As part of that, the 
US administration is proposing significant   changes to the federal budget—including 
cuts to science and Earth observation programs. Among them are NASA’s Orbiting 
Carbon Observatory missions, which monitor carbon emissions. The justification: 
while OCO-2 and OCO-3 are still functioning well and producing high-quality data, 
----------------Page (93) Break----------------
they were not designed to last this long—and cost-cutting is the priority. The debate 
has quickly become politicized, as reports in the media show. Yet a few facts remain: 
people elect officials to set policy, but making any country great requires science and 
technology. From far away, this seems a mismatch case. Data is essential for 
everything we do—personal decisions, business strategy, waging war or shaping 
policy. Without it, we’re flying blind. Earth monitoring underpins not just policy, but 
also business—NatureTech is a growing field. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
The US leads the world in Earth observation. That’s a fact. But if it steps back, others 
will fill the gap–guess who? Many nations understand that climate, weather and 
nature data are vital—and they see the opportunities to innovate and project 
influence globally. The real story here is about today’s global chess game, but also 
about how science can become politicized—a process that history shows intensifies    
during times of societal change or crisis. We are living through such a moment. 
Disbelief and politicization are spreading, and it may take time to reach a new steady 
state. In the meantime, I struggle to understand why the most advanced nation 
would voluntarily give up its edge in science and knowledge. Maybe I’m missing 
something. Ideas? 
That said, this past week Google released the amazing Alpha Planet—worth checking 
out.

 Dimming the Sun: Geoengineering is drawing 
capital, and controversy 
↳ Why it matters 
Venture capitalists are quietly moving into geoengineering—large-scale human 
interventions in Earth’s systems to counter climate change, from reflecting sunlight 
to artificially cooling the planet. Research agencies are paying attention too. This 
isn’t clean energy or circular economy. It’s planetary intervention—high-stakes, risky 
and until recently, unimaginable for most people. As climate tech tightens its belt, 
the pivot to geoengineering marks a shift from prevention to planetary patchwork. 
New business opportunities are emerging and money is starting to flow , but the risks 
of meddling with complex systems are immense. Public opinion remains skeptical. A 
recent Nature survey shows widespread distrust of these technologies and 
government action. The road ahead will be bumpy in our complex and frail world. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
This may sound like a sci-fi  plot, but conversations about geoengineering have been 
happening for decades. I remember debates in Silicon Valley back in 2013. Now, it’s 
coming out of the shadows. On one hand, the Earth is burning and options are 
shrinking. On the other, terraforming is not mitigation—it’s desperation. It addresses 
the effects but should never mean giving up on tackling the cause. What was once 
an extreme niche now involves active companies across Europe—in France, the 
Netherlands, the UK, Spain, Germany—and beyond, in Israel and China. China already 
runs the world’s largest “weather modification ” program with extensive cloud 
seeding. And like everything else, weather can be dual use. Some say the VC push is 
less about cooling the planet and more about owning the thermostat. What do you 
think?

Weekly Snapshot - Aug 31, 2025 
 
We live in a time when change takes many shapes. Sometimes through bold policies, 
sometimes through quiet technologies, and sometimes through societies breaking 
old cycles. 
This week, the signals come from very different corners: an African nation searching 
for a sustainable path from resources to prosperity, a laser technology opening new 
ways to understand and protect nature, and Mexico’s surprising leap in lifting 
millions out of poverty. 
Let’s dive in! 
----------------Page (96) Break----------------
 From opportunity to transformation: Africa in search 
of a model 
↳ Why it matters 
The Maputo International Trade Fair (FACIM) highlighted Mozambique’s push to 
attract foreign investment to create jobs, revive agriculture and rebuild after cyclones 
and ongoing conflict.  The task is daunting—and not unique to Mozambique. The 
country has natural gas, minerals, fertile land, water and young labor, but lacks 
capital, technical know-how (at scale), and large sophisticated business structures. 
Conflict  has already derailed a $20+ billion gas investment by Total. While critical 
resources exist, the capacity to turn them into growth remains limited. Capital looks 
for returns, but if it isn’t available locally there, why would someone choose 
Mozambique over competing opportunities elsewhere? Breaking these 
“non-material” constraints is essential not just for Mozambique, but for much of 
Africa’s development. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
A Mozambican leader said at FACIM: “If we have investment, we can solve all the 
issues.” Yes—but where will the money come from? And more broadly, how can 
developing nations turn resource wealth into sustainable growth? Minerals and 
energy can bring cash, but projects are complex and benefits   quite often don't reach 
beyond a small elite. Investment is oxygen, yet international capital often comes with 
strings, and debt can suffocate—Latin America remembers this well. Africa holds 
60% of the world’s remaining arable land and has a fast-growing population. Aid has 
failed as a path, but a scalable investment model is still missing. Multilaterals should 
help channel private capital into transformative projects. However, my 
non-statistically representative and anecdotal observations in places I know show 
that too often their “private sector” arms back the same safe, low-risk ventures that 
private funds already finance. Is that really their mission? 
----------------Page (97) Break----------------

 Nature: We should know it better 
↳ Why it matters 
MIT Technology Review spotlighted this past week how “Lidar”—an acronym for the 
laser-based mapping tech best known for self-driving cars, meaning light detection 
and ranging—is now being deployed to measure, model and mitigate climate change 
risks, from flood prediction to wildfir e spread. Its precision could help governments 
and insurers prepare better, potentially saving billions in disaster costs. But this is 
just the tip of the iceberg. The truth is that while we know a lot about human and 
consumer behavior (Facebook probably knows more about you than you do, dear 
reader), we know very little about nature and the environments where we live. 
Technology must be used to close this gap. As the climate changes, we will 
increasingly need to understand its impacts on the environment and human 
activities. We also need data to be better prepared to “create value from 
nature”—forests, species, biomes, water sources and more. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
A technology designed for mobility is becoming essential for survival and the 
functioning of the economy at a larger scale. Lidar and similar technologies won’t 
stop disasters, but they can help communities turn surprise into foresight and 
preparedness. Other applications must follow—we need to measure nature to 
prepare, to create value and to preserve. Today, we are losing species we don’t even 
know exist–Imagine you inherit a bundle of things from a wealthy and generous 
----------------Page (98) Break----------------
family member, and you throw it away without even knowing what's inside; would you 
do that? Yet we still lack, at scale, the commercial technologies required to measure 
and understand nature. That is why initiatives such as the XPrize Rainforest are so 
important. As these efforts grow, new business models, companies and even entire 
industry verticals will emerge.I feel tremendously excited about companies in which I 
have directly or indirectly invested, such as YangPlanet and Wyvern. The sustainable 
future—we’ll build it.

 Mexico moves society forward: What is needed to 
take the next step? 
↳ Why it matters 
Mexico recorded a historic drop in poverty, with nearly 9 million people lifted above 
the poverty line since 2018. Social welfare policies, higher minimum wages and 
economic resilience all played a role. The achievement is politically significant   for 
Claudia Sheinbaum’s government and could reshape the narrative of Latin America’s 
fight against inequality. But what is needed to keep that going? The only truly 
sustainable path for countries to move to a higher level goes through maximizing the 
use of their most precious (so far) resource: human capital. To do that, they need to 
train and educate people. To continue this trajectory, Mexico and others must 
simultaneously: (i) train and educate people with quality, speed and scale to avoid 
‘losing the window’; (ii) fund that effort without compromising other critical areas, 
and (iii) do it in a time of massive transformation, when skills are shifting, AI is 
----------------Page (99) Break----------------
shaking the foundations and many jobs are vanishing while others are being created 
in real time. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
Inequality in Latin America is tremendous and continues to grow, stressing and 
challenging social cohesion—in Brazil alone, the income of the richest 0.1% rose 
more than five times the general average between 2017 and 2023. While there is 
debate about whether the world is heading toward a “haves vs. have-nots” or “haves 
and have-lots” reality, the fact remains: the lack of investment in human capabilities, 
relative to population potential, is holding back future development. Social policies 
are important, but not enough. Latin America and other parts of the world must 
focus on building stronger, competitive economies—and that is ultimately a function 
of talent. 
Not convinced? Consider this: Brazil graduates about 1.38 million people per year, 
but only around 220,000 in STEM—I know other areas matter, but let's be frank: 
engineers are building the future AI models, computer chips and chip fabrication 
manufacturing that will determine economic fates. China, on the other hand, 
graduates 3.6 million STEM students annually—16 times more, far beyond the 6–7 
times ratio between the two populations. The gap is widening, fast. 
Mexico’s story matters globally: if sustained, it could become a model of inclusive 
development in an era of rising inequality. But only a handful of countries have truly 
succeeded in scaling up quality education and moving citizens from the liabilities to 
the assets column of their social balance sheets. Can Mexico pull that off? How? 
Can AI help? How?

Weekly Snapshot - Aug 24, 2025 
 
 From Free Markets to Fee Markets: Intel Inside 
Washington 
↳ Why it matters 
Two days ago, the U.S. government crossed a new threshold. After decades of 
advocating free markets, Washington announced it will take a 10% stake in 
Intel—making the U.S. government the main shareholder and turning a cornerstone 
of America’s tech industry into a partial state-owned enterprise. Axios called this 
approach, which among other things includes charging 15% of AMD and Nvidia sales 
abroad, “pay-me capitalism”: subsidies and support tied to strings like equity stakes, 
----------------Page (101) Break----------------
royalties or revenue-sharing–or simply ‘loyalty’ to the administration's agenda. Once 
reserved for bailouts in crises—like the U.S. government takeover of GM in the 
aftermath of the 2008 financial crisis, when it acquired more than 60% of the “new 
GM”—the U.S. government is now proactively embedding itself in strategic sectors. 
Semiconductors are the opening act... could energy, biotech and space be next? This 
marks a fundamental shift that will impact the U.S. economy and send signals 
across the world—not to mention Intel itself. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
This is a fundamental rewriting of the American economic playbook and, particularly, 
the American narrative. Forget the invisible hand; it is in plain sight now. The state is 
no longer just a regulator or funder; it is again positioning itself as a shareholder, 
with direct influence over industries central to national security and global 
competition. 
Trump got it right: controlling critical technologies—what could be more important 
than semiconductors?—is essential in the geostrategic competition with China and 
beyond. It is also a clear recognition of the limits of the market economy and a 
display of the will of the U.S. administration to command the economy in a very 
visible way. Will it work for the U.S. and Intel? To be seen—make your bets. 
The case may become the emblem of the U.S. ‘adapting’ state-guided capitalism to 
its own context. But the U.S. is not China. And countries across the world are not the 
U.S. China is an extreme case in its capacity to command the economy, with a 
different reality and levers to direct it. The U.S., on the other hand, is exceptional in 
terms of its tech capacity, resources and dynamism—is it immune to government 
intervention?—and the capabilities of its economy. Nevertheless, intellectual 
curiosity aside, what really worries me is how other nations will read this. Others may 
want to copy the model—business, politics and policy love fads and trends—without 
understanding the issues behind it. The American and Chinese governments, despite 
their vast differences, have capabilities, resources and freedom to act in ways that 
----------------Page (102) Break----------------
autarkic and bureaucratic governments, where public and private actors follow 
separate legal regimes, simply do not–yes, I am thinking about a certain nation in the 
tropics. Copying these models will not work in old-fashioned institutional 
environments with obsolete toolkits. Stay alert for what is coming to your own reality 
folks. 
↳ Read more here 
http://bit.ly/4oLQvnD 
http://bit.ly/3UG3KbQ 
http://bit.ly/47Jdsld

Europe is burning—literally. Heatwaves have reached record highs across the

costs will be enormous. But there are also opportunities—for innovators and

 The disruption of Economics: We Are Not Equipped

↳ Why it matters 
Two apparently unrelated headlines caught my attention this week. Technology 
economist Carlotta Perez warned of a potential AI bubble, and U.S. Fed Chair Jerome

but they’re likely intertwined. AI has triggered unprecedented levels of capital 
investment in digital infrastructure—yet ROI remains elusive for most firms. At the 
same time, job markets are showing early signs of disruption. Openings for IT roles 
are declining in the U.S., and some companies are already building products without 
coding teams. AI is enabling that. Tech cycles always bring booms and busts—but 
----------------Page (104) Break----------------
this time, the stakes are higher. Job displacement, especially among white-collar 
workers, is coming fast. Forecasting based on past time series won’t help. 
Economics isn’t equipped for this kind of shift. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
Unemployment among recent U.S. college grads is now above 6%—higher than the 
4.2% national average. The point: AI is advancing fast, but education systems are 
still training people for yesterday’s jobs. Career promises tied to technical skills are 
no longer delivering. Meanwhile, online influencers inspire millions. For better or 
worse, AI and the internet empower individuals to build and create like never before. 
But most people—and most institutions—are seeing these changes only through the 
rearview mirror. 
Back to jobs, is Powell seeing something that most aren’t—and that data isn’t 
showing yet? Organizations and economies need to level up their strategic 
capabilities, invest in foresight and build a broader understanding of what’s 
unfolding. Few countries and companies have that. Even fewer are aware they need 
it. Buckle up.

Weekly Snapshot - Aug 16, 2025

 A hacker may be coming for your dreams 
↳ Why it matters 
Physiology’s next great leap is happening inside our heads. Recent research has 
advanced both our ability to “listen to” and “maintain” the brain. A new implant can 
decode a person’s “internal speech” (yes... what you are thinking) with up to 74% 
accuracy, but only after the user thinks of a preset password (ufff...). Using 
CRISPR-based tools, researchers corrected mutations, improved cognition and 
reduced symptoms of devastating brain disorders in mice—bringing brain-targeted 
therapies closer to human trials. The implications range from new treatments for 
neurological disorders—and big business—to a deeper understanding of 
consciousness itself. And yes, pretty soon someone may be able to read your 
dreams. 
----------------Page (106) Break----------------
↳ My thoughts on it 
We are living in an age of technological convergence. New materials and sensors 
can now read data from our brains. AI can help us decode patterns and interpret 
those signals. Synthetic biology—powered by AI and computation—can edit genes 
and re-engineer molecules that interact with the brain. All of this is deeply connected, 
and the impacts will be tremendous. Just think about it—and be mindful. Someone 
may be listening. 
Questions abound. Who owns your dreams? Are you the owner of your thoughts? 
Your mind? Your consciousness? This opens a wide area for work on privacy rights, 
existential rights, and blurs the very notion of the self. What happens when your brain 
is uploaded to an AI? Would that be a clone of you? Would it have will? As we push 
these frontiers, new fields like neural rights are fast emerging.  While this may sound 
far-fetched, the developments we saw just this past week are strong signals that this 
future is much closer than we think. Have you thought about it?

 Countries need solid-grounded future plans 
↳ Why it matters 
The demand for rare earth minerals is soaring. They are essential for manufacturing 
electronics, renewable energy technologies and nearly everything 
computer-related—and the demand will only grow. China controls around 60–70% of 
production and about 90% of refining, giving the country enormous leverage. China 
has learned to coordinate its mining and manufacturing sectors to manage global 
----------------Page (107) Break----------------
flows, just as the U.S. tries to choke China on semiconductors. Rare earths are 
China’s counterweight. 
REMs are a major geostrategic topic, and countries such as Japan are exploring 
ways to increase local supply. Japan recently launched a joint initiative with the 
European Union under which they will pursue joint public–private procurement of 
rare earth elements. At the same time, they are exploring seabed 
production—despite major environmental concerns. This REM rush opens up 
business opportunities: developing new deposits, scaling up refining capacity, 
investing in recycling (where Europe leads), developing technology and even 
exploring sea sources. Expect news, including from outer space. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
The 2010 China–Japan incident was an early warning of China’s dominance. Fifteen 
years later, most countries are still unprepared to compete—or to seize the 
upside—but that may be changing. Japan is seeking to secure independence in an 
era where supply chains can be weaponized. This is a pre-emptive strike in the 
resource geopolitics game: diversify supply now, or be vulnerable later. At the same 
time, the U.S., China, Japan and about a dozen more countries are moving on sea 
mining. Europe is doubling down on circular strategies. And players such as Brazil 
are stepping into the refining segment. We’re seeing a deep geopolitical shift, and 
what surprises me is to find a country that does not have a focus on REMs yet.

Weekly Snapshot - Aug 11, 2025 
 
 3D printing on the Moon is coming. Who will build 
first? 
↳ Why it matters Last Friday, Chinese researchers made the news in Western media 
with a solar-powered device that concentrates light and melts lunar regolith (moon 
dirt), which can then be used to 3D print objects, structures and infrastructure on the 
Moon. Establishing a permanent presence on the Moon is a challenge, and existing 
solutions require transporting building materials from Earth. When operational, 
devices using this new technology could significantly  lower the cost and complexity 
of building habitats on the Moon. The country that masters it will have an edge in the 
race for the Moon. We may have just had a glimpse of a cornerstone for sustained 
lunar presence, moving from idea to prototype. 
↳ My thoughts on it Space is a major frontier for exploration, tech development, 
business and geopolitical competition. The technological offshoots of space 
programs are well-documented, and countries that succeed in space exploration can 
build a competitive edge. But all space-related activities are risky and 
capital-intensive. At its height, the Apollo Project accounted for 2.2% of the U.S. 
federal budget. Not many countries can have big, bold ambitions in space. Although 
India has succeeded in putting in place the most cost-effective program in the 
----------------Page (110) Break----------------
world—kudos to them—that is the exception and, in any scenario, being 
cost-effective is imperative. When we think about scale in space activities, logistics 
is key. At Seldor Capital, we mapped critical needs for space—storing data, 
processing data, manufacturing goods, building infrastructure. Solutions that enable 
these capabilities while skipping logistics from Earth will be big wins.

 GPT-5 takes UX to a new level. But we are still just in

↳ Why it matters OpenAI has officially launched GPT-5, its most advanced language

and more. GPT-5 consolidates multiple models under one intelligent system with a 
real-time router that chooses between fast responses and deep reasoning depending 
on the task. It dramatically reduces hallucinations (by 45–80%) and delivers far more 
factual, reliable output than previous models. Importantly, it introduces new safety 
systems to minimize deceptive or sycophantic behavior, aligning better with purpose 
and truth. It is a big step forward in making AI smoother and more reliable from a 
user perspective—others will follow. But... it is not Artificial General Intelligence. Or is 
it? 
↳ My thoughts on it The seamless blend of speed, accuracy, reasoning and 
adaptability transforms the user experience at every level. Having a single 
----------------Page (111) Break----------------
interface—no model swapping anymore—makes a big difference, doesn’t it? Think

beyond the knowledge of the person you’re talking to. OpenAI’s intent reflects a 
grander ambition related to AGI. Don’t be surprised—the impacts will be massive for 
white-collar jobs and work, and things are changing as we speak. But let me tell you 
what really surprises me: people in intellectual jobs who are not yet using AI. Why?
