 AI in the Boardroom. Are organizations ready for 
this tech era?
 
↳ Why it matters This past week, new data revealed a sharp shift in how corporate 
boards are managing AI. According to Ernst & Young’s proxy-season review, the 
number of S&P 500 companies disclosing AI oversight committees more than tripled 
in 2025, with nearly half of Fortune 100 boards now citing AI as a required 
qualification  for directors (up from 26% in 2024). Meanwhile, a report from 
AuditBoard warns that AI deployment is outpacing governance, with only 25% of 
organizations having fully implemented AI governance programs despite widespread 
AI use. These dynamics show boards scrambling to catch up, but not necessarily 
keeping pace effectively. As in other areas of management, the big issue is how to 
address the challenge and improve performance without inflating costs or 
----------------Page (112) Break----------------
jeopardizing results. Simply adding AI governance structures will hardly be the 
answer. 
↳ My thoughts on it We’re witnessing boards wake up to the strategic urgency of AI, 
but execution is still nascent, and the usual risks of building structures and copying 
management practices without truly strategizing or understanding the situation are 
always present. None of this is new in business. What is new is the unprecedented 
speed of change. It’s not enough to name committees; boards must embed AI 
fluency into decision-making, accountability frameworks and risk monitoring—but all 
of this will only make sense as part of a broader strategic approach powered by 
effective organizational solutions. In a week, the headlines change—the AI headlines 
won’t vanish, though; it’s here to stay—and shortsighted solutions will not suffice.  In 
times of fast-paced change, strategic capabilities have never been more important.

 Making advanced computer chips: a feat far from 
easy 
↳ Why it matters 
Semiconductors are essential in today's world. TSMC alone produces at least 90% of 
the world’s most advanced chips, all concentrated in Taiwan. Any disruption—a war, 
earthquake or pandemic—would impact the entire world. The U.S. is working to 
rebuild local manufacturing capacity but that is far from easy. This past week, U.S. 
Secretary Scott Bessent noted how difficult  it has been to get TSMC’s $42 billion 
Arizona project operational, pointing to local and state regulations (and they are 
enforced) as obstacles. Building advanced tech manufacturing at scale is a long and 
complex process and the basics of competitiveness matter. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
Taiwan is an extraordinary success story and TSMC is its symbol. From agriculture 
to a global tech powerhouse, Taiwan was built on investment in talent and science 
and the build-up of business capabilities. I saw it first-hand visiting ITRI, TSMC, and 
working with ITRI colleagues. Bessent’s comments are a reminder: high tech is not 
just about labs. You need talent, infrastructure, and a low-friction business 
environment, especially in a time of fast-paced change. Also: balancing regulation, 
citizen protection, and competitiveness is tough—my take: AI can help a lot. That 
said, the U.S. 20% tariffs imposed on Taiwan this past week sent TSMC’s stock 
plunging. Planned? Thought through? In an increasingly complex world, many things 
seem not quite right.

 AI is hungry and privacy is dead: most people didn't 
notice 
↳ Why it matters 
This past week, reports revealed that Google's search engine was indexing content 
provided by individual users on ChatGPT and making it available to others searching 
the web. In the same week, the NYT announced it had reached an agreement with 
Amazon, allowing the tech giant to feed NYT's content into its LLM in exchange for 
an annual fee of $20 million. AI is hungry. Media platforms are worried – a few, like 
the NYT, are making deals. In the meantime, ChatGPT users were accidentally 
making their private conversations discoverable by search engines such as Google, 
turning personal dialogues into public web results. Sensitive content including 
therapy-style exchanges, resumes, emails, and even internal corporate insights 
surfaced via Google searches. Most people have not noticed (have you?), but privacy 
is a mirage in today's world. And asymmetries in the relationships with tech giants 
are significant.  
↳ My thoughts on it 
AI runs on data—period. And that data comes from everywhere. Those fine-print 
terms we rarely read when signing up for services? That’s often where we give 
permission for our data to be used. In reality, we share personal details—our work, 
habits and even family information—across countless platforms. Two things are 
worth keeping in mind. First, we will increasingly see fights and deals related to data 
access. Surely, that will happen in the B2C world. Second, data privacy as we once 
knew it is largely gone. With so many platforms and ways for data to be sold, 
licensed or leaked, control is nearly impossible. Most people don’t fully grasp the 
business models behind the services they use. Increasingly, these models revolve 
around turning our data into a product.

market failures? A lack of strategy and vision from Western nations? We are still at 
the beginning of this race, but a future with bifurcated digital infrastructures—one 
largely Western, one Asia-led—should not surprise anyone.

 From water to bits: China builds a mega-dam to 
power AI 
↳ Why it matters 
This past week, China has officially begun building what will be the world’s largest 
hydropower dam, deep in the Tibetan plateau, on the Yarlung Tsangpo river. The 
project—expected to cost $167 billion—would surpass the Three Gorges Dam and 
generate three times more energy. But it’s not just about electricity. The river flows 
into India and Bangladesh, meaning China’s control over it raises geopolitical, 
----------------Page (117) Break----------------
environmental, and humanitarian alarms. India fears the dam could become a “water 
bomb,” while Bangladesh has requested transparency. For Beijing, this is a strategic 
energy move. For its neighbors, it’s an existential threat. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
Vladimir Putin once said that “whoever controls AI will control the world." This case 
adds a regional layer—with potential global consequences—to the equation: whoever 
controls the flow  of water from the Tibetan plateau holds immense geopolitical 
power. China and its neighbors know it. With this project, the long-contested Tibetan 
territory gains highlighted relevance—regionally and globally. Water is becoming the 
new oil in Asia, and this megadam may set a dangerous precedent. In a world 
already shaped by energy and AI disputes, competition (and wars...) over water may 
also be on the rise. 
↳ Read more here 
https://tinyurl.com/5cs56hs7 
 Money Dries Up for Biotech in California — A Great 
Time to Invest (Wisely) 
↳ Why it matters 
Biotech in the San Francisco Bay Area is undergoing a downturn: widespread layoffs, 
company closures and tanking valuations are now commonplace. The Nasdaq 
Biotechnology Index has tumbled nearly 30% in 2025, and nearly 60% of biotech 
firms reportedly have less than two years of funding runway. Combined with cuts to 
NIH support and tightening venture capital, this downturn may delay key drug 
developments and reshape the innovation ecosystem. But... this is also a great 
opportunity to invest! Pay attention! 
↳ My thoughts on it 
----------------Page (118) Break----------------
Biotech is a high-risk, high-return, high-volatility industry—where else do you find a 
stock jumping almost 4,000% in a single day? It’s also deeply connected to science 
investment. Promising pipelines now risk death by financial attrition... and here is a 
big opportunity for those who are cashed in and have the capabilities to assess 
teams and their portfolios. 
This reality also underscores how policy shifts around public research funding can 
ripple through the innovation chain and impact the entire sector. But look—a HSBC 
report released about two weeks ago highlights that “the number of funding deals 
involving drug candidates from China continued to climb, meanwhile...” Wow! What 
does this mean? Here’s one hypothesis: biotech has a really loaded cost structure in 
the US—high salaries, fancy offices,  long and expensive clinical trials, huge 
compliance costs. Startups coming from other markets can look more attractive to 
investors right now. It doesn’t have to be China. Think Australia—with amazing talent 
and an integrated regulatory environment with the US—or Brazil, which has 
world-class science talent eager for opportunities to create global value. It may be 
time to think differently and globally.

↳ My thoughts on it

possible, inequality could take on a whole new dimension. The wealthy already have

divide becomes genetic. Does that sound strange or frightening?

Just think: advanced economies already differ from the least developed ones in 
terms of capital availability, institutional quality, stocks of capabilities (in 
manufacturing, design, research, finance, marketing, etc.), the ability to provide 
quality education and, ultimately, talent pools. Genetics has not been part of that 
----------------Page (123) Break----------------
divide—yet. But that could soon change. Radically. Are we ready for that? And if not, 
how do we prepare?

 We are at the verge of a bio-knowledge explosion 
↳ Why it matters 
We are entering the era of a bio-knowledge explosion. Since decoding the human 
genome in 2003, progress in health hasn’t matched the expectations many had—but 
----------------Page (124) Break----------------
that’s about to change. A convergence of genomics, AI, synthetic biology and 
computing is now unlocking breakthroughs. This past week, news from China broke 
about scientists identifying the gene set that enables complex tissue regeneration 
and manipulating it to switch on the regenerative capacity of tissue—a trait that 
salamanders have but mammals do not. Add to this the race to model “virtual cells,” 
as featured in Nature a few days ago, and you can sense a shift: we’re moving from 
sequencing life to simulating and engineering it. The explosion of bio-knowledge and 
its applications will revolutionize health, biology and possibly even our definition of 
life. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
Yes, humanity has made a lot of progress. But... from the heights of the ignorance on 
the matter conferred to me by my engineering education, I’ve long seen medicine as 
powerful but still primitive. False positives, misdiagnoses, limited understanding of 
cellular machinery—we know so little yet about how we and life work. Now, the 
fusion of high-performance computing (quantum soon to be added to the list), 
genomics (and other ‘omics’), advanced visualization tools (for instance, a BSL-4 
biolab connected to a particle accelerator), AI and advanced biotech tools like 
CRISPR is opening a window into the engines of life. We are just scratching the 
surface of this revolution. What’s coming next? A real explosion of biological 
knowledge—and with it, a transformation in how we understand, manipulate and 
design life itself.

 Deep Tech in the U.S. in 2025: $60.2B and Counting

breakthroughs in materials, biology, hardware, computation and energy—think

businesses and governments are increasingly aligned. Have you noticed that? To my 
Brazilian friends: U.S. VC investment in deep tech so far in 2025 is 20 times greater 
than the total VC investment in Brazil in all of 2024. The overall U.S. VC market is at

Weekly Snapshot - Jun 16, 2025

 Climate-responsive, color-changing paint 
↳ Why it matters 
The development by a U.S. industrial designer of a thermochromic “climate-adaptive” 
paint that shifts hue based on temperature—turning dark in winter to absorb heat and 
light in summer to reflect it—made headlines this past week. The paint reacts above 
approximately 25 °C (77 °F), transitioning from a tinted color to white, and reverses 
as temperatures drop. Heating and cooling of buildings account for around 25% of 
global energy consumption. It is also one of the fastest-growing categories—there 
are 2 billion AC units worldwide, and with rising incomes in emerging nations and 
increasing temperatures, sales are growing at a 6–7% CAGR. Tests suggest this 
----------------Page (132) Break----------------
paint could reduce a building’s heating and cooling energy use by 20–30% annually. 
Do the math, apply a large discount, and you’ll still see how important this could be. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
Joe Doucet is a well-known American designer. His design practice, JDXP, claims to 
work at the intersection of design, innovation and sustainability. As someone who 
truly believes in the power of design and the need to solve problems by design, I 
loved this. Where did the inspiration for the “climate-adaptive” paint come from? 
Mood rings, according to them. The paint hasn’t reached the market yet, and its 
durability and cost will need to pass the test of consumer preferences. Above all, this 
case highlights the potential for innovation in materials. Mostly unseen and often 
misunderstood, materials are at the core of today’s economy. With AI, computation, 
synbio and other technologies converging rapidly, we can expect a wave of 
innovation in this space. 
↳ Read more here 
● http://bit.ly/4n4E0Ti 
● http://bit.ly/44kuEeQ 
● http://bit.ly/43M4O3i 
● http://bit.ly/4efDu0P 
 Riders in the sky: the space race heats up 
↳ Why it matters 
The space race is heating up, and new reports this past week point to scenarios that 
key competitors are actively preparing for. Countries like the U.S. and China appear 
to be escalating their readiness for “orbital dogfights,” anticipating hostile satellite 
maneuvers, anti-satellite weapons and drone swarms in orbit. Strategic priorities 
now include protecting satellite networks like Starlink, defending against 
space-based nuclear threats, building orbital missile defenses and responding to 
----------------Page (133) Break----------------
direct threats to assets in space—such as space stations. This marks a pivotal shift: 
outer space is rapidly becoming the next contested domain in great-power rivalry, 
and international law is crumbling before our eyes. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
Space warfare used to belong to the realm of science fiction—it doesn’t anymore. 
Life on Earth now depends heavily on space capabilities and assets. Securing 
satellite systems is no longer just a military priority; it’s essential to modern 
society—from banking to disaster response. One reality brought into plain sight by 
the war in Ukraine is the integration of commercial platforms (e.g., Starlink) into 
national defense. Although only a handful of countries have the capacity to address 
this, space security has become a critical issue for nations worldwide. Combined 
with the promise of space mining and the potential for space-based 
biomanufacturing, this will only accelerate the ongoing drive for space exploration 
and the emerging space economy. 
↳ Read more here 
● http://bit.ly/3G1NlKZ 
● http://bit.ly/4e5QWnN 
 A boom in global carbon-capture investment 
↳ Why it matters 
Sustainability and climate science may no longer feel as fashionable in many parts 
of the world as they did just months ago. Still, governments and enterprises are 
channeling a record $80 billion into carbon capture and storage (CCS) projects by 
2030—a fourfold increase in capacity, according to a new report released last week. 
CCS is emerging as a critical tool to decarbonize hard-to-abate sectors like cement, 
steel, and power generation. This surge reflects growing net-zero ambitions ahead of 
COP30, signaling both new investment opportunities and a broader shift from 
----------------Page (134) Break----------------
speculative to scalable climate solutions. Not just climate activists—but especially 
investors—should be paying close attention. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
It’s increasingly clear that the Paris Agreement emission targets won’t be met. If 
cutting emissions isn’t enough, can carbon storage become a scalable business? 
Momentum is growing. Nature still offers the best carbon capture method, but 
scaling NBS projects—especially in the Global South—faces some serious social and 
institutional barriers. CCS is advancing faster, in part because it avoids those 
constraints. Still, investment tells a story: in 2024, VC funding for energy tech was 
just a third of what went into AI—down from parity in 2023. And what do we do with 
captured carbon? Companies like Cemvita have answers. Interestingly, Occidental 
Petroleum—featured in the CCS report that sparked this post—is also backing 
Cemvita. 
↳ Read more here

 The US-China Tech War Goes Deeper 
↳ Why it matters 
This week, Synopsys, a leading U.S. chip design software firm, ordered its China 
teams to halt all sales and services, suspending new orders. The decision follows 
fresh U.S. export controls on chip design tools and semiconductor materials, 
targeting Chinese clients, local staff of global firms, and military-linked users. 
Together with Cadence and Siemens EDA, Synopsys controls over 70% of China’s 
chip design software market. Cutting access further strains China’s semiconductor 
industry, already under pressure from earlier sanctions. The move escalates the 
U.S.-China tech conflict  and sends a broader warning: relying on foreign technology 
in critical areas can pose serious risks. Few countries can build local 
alternatives—many wish they could. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
----------------Page (136) Break----------------
This feels like another chess move in the long game between Washington and 
Beijing. It’s hard to strike a balance, as restrictions like these don’t necessarily stop 
innovation—they often accelerate domestic alternatives. Timing is key. Chinese EDA 
firms like Empyrean and Primarius are gaining momentum, and reports of pirated 
EDA software are surfacing too. And let’s be honest: China was expecting this. It’s 
almost certain they’ve been ramping up investment and support for indigenous 
players, and I wouldn’t be surprised if they have an ace up their sleeve. Synopsys will 
likely take a short-term hit—China represented a significant   share of its revenue. 
Who’s going to absorb that loss? But the long game remains uncertain, and 
outcomes are hard to predict—place your bets. Right now, every tool, every line of 
code, is a battleground. 
↳ Read more here 
https://bit.ly/43wQH1z 
https://bit.ly/4kUblP2 
 Pride Month 2025: Corporate Support (and Values) 
Wanes 
↳ Why it matters 
Pride events across the U.S. are facing major budget gaps this year as corporate 
sponsors pull back. A Gravity Research survey shows that 39% of companies plan to 
scale down public Pride Month support, fearing backlash over DEI initiatives. Cities 
like San Francisco and New York report shortfalls of up to $750,000. Companies 
including Comcast, Anheuser-Busch and PepsiCo have cut or ended support, citing 
political pressure and rising anti-LGBTQ+ sentiment under Trump. Some now donate 
anonymously. Pride groups are turning to grassroots fundraising to fill the gaps. Free 
market? Here’s the truth: companies are highly sensitive to the political 
environment—and, with a very few exceptions, they bend to the will of the powers 
that be. 
----------------Page (137) Break----------------
↳ My thoughts on it 
The withdrawal of corporate sponsorships from Pride events is a stark reminder of 
how fragile corporate values really are. In most cases, they’re nothing more than 
empty PR—pure performance, total BS. To be fair, the U.S. government is incredibly 
powerful, and navigating the political landscape isn’t easy for any company. That 
said, as Pride events return to their grassroots origins, two key questions emerge. 
First, can individuals step up and keep the momentum alive? Second, can a cause 
stay strong when it becomes dependent on corporate funding to survive? 
↳ Read more here 
https://shorturl.at/YzeeQ

 Ukraine's Drone Offensive Redefines   Modern 
Warfare 
↳ Why it matters 
On June 1, 2025, Ukraine executed "Operation Spiderweb," a meticulously planned 
drone offensive targeting five Russian airbases deep inside Russian territory. 
Utilizing 117 first-person view (FPV) drones launched from concealed trucks within 
Russia, the operation reportedly damaged or destroyed more than 40 Russian 
military aircraft, including strategic bombers such as the Tu-95, Tu-22M3 and A-50 
airborne early warning and control planes. Some analysts estimate the attack 
caused around $7 billion in damages and compromised roughly 34% of Russia’s 
strategic cruise missile carriers stationed at airbases. This unprecedented strike not 
only demonstrated Ukraine’s growing capabilities in asymmetric warfare but, more 
importantly, revealed the transformative impact of drone technology and a 
fundamental shift in the nature of warfare: attacking has become much cheaper than 
defending. 
----------------Page (138) Break----------------
↳ My thoughts on it 
How do you protect a $2 billion B-2 Spirit from 1,000 drones that cost just $2,000 
each? The successful use of low-cost, commercially available drones to incapacitate 
high-value military assets marks a turning point in modern warfare. The ability of a 
smaller nation or even a non-state actor to inflict serious damage on a much larger 
adversary has reached a new level. From force design to industrial policy, 
fundamental questions are now on the table. This kind of operation not only 
challenges traditional ideas of military superiority, it also serves as a wake-up call for 
nations everywhere to rethink their defense strategies in light of the democratization 
of drone technology. Where do the US, EU and China keep their most valuable 
aircraft? What about other critical assets? I bet many high-ranking officers  in 
advanced economies are staying up all night right now, trying to prepare for 
scenarios they never imagined. 
↳ Read more here 
https://shorturl.at/7C4ph 
https://bit.ly/45LFf3A

 Water security takes center stage in Astana (and 
worldwide) 
↳ Why it matters 
This past week at the Astana International Forum (AIF) 2025, leaders and experts 
emphasized the urgent need for regional cooperation in Central Asia to address 
water scarcity and climate challenges. Shared rivers like the Syr Darya and Amu 
Darya, which flow  across the region into the Aral Sea, are under growing pressure 
due to inefficient  use, outdated infrastructure and rising demand from agriculture, 
industry and population growth. Water is often taken for granted in wealthier or 
naturally endowed regions, but it is an essential resource that is increasingly in short 
supply. In Kazakhstan alone, 500,000 people lack access to water. The Aral Sea, 
once the fourth-largest lake in the world, has dried up by 92 percent since 1960. The 
lack of water kills, stalls economies and triggers conflict—not just in Central Asia. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
----------------Page (140) Break----------------
The other day I heard from a colleague: “ when water ends, war starts.”. So true. 
Water is Water is essential—especially in geopolitically sensitive regions like Central 
Asia. Consider India and Pakistan: 80% of Pakistan’s drinking and irrigation water 
comes from rivers governed by the Indus Water Treaty with India. Signed in the 
1960s, the treaty has withstood multiple conflicts  but was challenged by India during 
recent clashes. Globally, 1.1 billion people lack access to safe water, and 2.7 billion 
face water scarcity for at least one month each year. Water isn’t just about drought 
or irrigation—it’s about power, borders and long-term stability. When natural 
resources cross man-made borders, so do the risks. Global freshwater stocks are 
rapidly declining, and 2023 was the driest year for rivers worldwide in over three 
decades. Things are getting hot and the clock is ticking. 
↳ Read more here 
https://bit.ly/3Z7JvXg 
https://bit.ly/4jlF2Hr 
https://bit.ly/4kM10EJ

↳ My thoughts on it

are high. First, this is part of a multi-trillion-dollar dispute between national 
governments and big tech, with Europe leading the effort to curb the influence of 
tech giants and assert digital tech sovereignty. Second, despite—or perhaps also 
because of—its regulatory push, Europe has lagged behind in digital innovation and 
competitiveness. Third, moves like Germany’s may further strain transatlantic 
relations, especially with the U.S., where most of these companies are based. A

 Tiny Passengers, Big Changes: The Evolution of 
Spaceborne Life 
↳ Why it matters 
Chinese scientists have discovered a previously unknown bacterial strain aboard the 
Tiangong space station. The new microbe, Arthrobacter changlensis, was isolated 
from a surface sample taken in 2022 and is notably adapted to harsh 
environments—radiation, low temperatures and microgravity. Bacteria in space is not 
new, but this strain appears to have undergone significant   adaptation, developing 
mechanisms to protect itself from the cellular degradation caused by radiation. This 
discovery could provide valuable clues on how to better prepare humans for 
long-haul space missions. 
----------------Page (144) Break----------------
↳ My thoughts on it 
Understanding cellular mutation in space is deeply strategic. As nations race to 
colonize orbital and lunar environments, knowing how life adapts in space isn’t just 
scientific   curiosity—it’s infrastructure planning. The more nations competing in the 
space arena advance their biological space research, the more self-sufficient   and 
sovereign their future off-Earth operations will become. I bet NASA, ESA and others 
are also paying close attention. 
↳ Read more here 
https://bit.ly/4dAniXS 
https://bit.ly/3Sg5FTg 
 Big Pharma Just Bought Your Family Tree 
↳ Why it matters 
Biotech giant Regeneron is acquiring the bankrupt consumer DNA company 
23andMe for $256 million. The deal includes 23andMe’s genetic database of over 14 
million individuals—including myself. This is one of the largest privately held pools of 
genomic data in the world. While 23andMe failed to sustain its direct-to-consumer 
business, its vast trove of health data is now being absorbed into a pharmaceutical 
R&D engine. Its downfall is a powerful story of how even the ‘best’ and most 
well-resourced companies can become trapped in their own business models and 
fail to adapt. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
Health data is the new oil. But monetizing that data isn’t as easy as it once seemed, 
as 23andMe’s downfall shows. With this move, Regeneron didn’t just acquire a failed 
company—it acquired a shortcut to personalized medicine. This is a textbook case of 
vertical integration. 23andMe’s data alone wasn’t enough to generate the cash flow  
----------------Page (145) Break----------------
needed to sustain the company as a standalone operation, but those same data 
assets can (potentially) unlock massive value when integrated into the structure of a 
pharmaceutical giant. The future of pharma won’t be built in labs alone, but in the 
data trails we handed over while spitting into test tubes. And frankly, it wouldn’t 
surprise me if news eventually emerges about the role the U.S. government played in 
catalyzing this deal. 
↳ Read more here 
https://bit.ly/3FmhQuM 
https://bit.ly/3HqtEN6

 What do you read every day? Why? 
↳ Why it matters 
News emerged this past week that changes in Google and YouTube’s algorithms 
have significantly  reduced the visibility of Turkish independent media and critics of 
Erdoğan’s government. This goes beyond simple SEO tweaking—it raises questions 
about who controls the voices in digital spaces. I’ll tell you a secret: it’s not you who 
chooses what you read. Our choices about the content we consume are mostly 
shaped and controlled by social media algorithms and the interests that govern 
those. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
Whether intentional or not, the shifts in Turkey highlight the disturbing concentration 
of power in a handful of platforms that increasingly act as invisible editors of public 
discourse. To be fair, this isn’t new. Traditional media is incredibly concentrated—and 
used to be even more so in the past. Social media platforms are not neutral (by the 
way, nothing in the world is truly neutral); they are driven by economic motives—to 
maximize engagement and profit for the platform—and political motivations, to 
support or undermine certain viewpoints. And let’s be clear—companies aren’t 
neutral either. They have their own interests and often bend to or align with powerful 
ones, in Turkey and everywhere. By the way: why are you reading this?

 Spain's Hispasat Eyes Ukraine’s Frontline 
↳ Why it matters 
Spanish satellite operator Hispasat is considering offering coverage to the Ukrainian 
military. The move comes amid growing concerns that Starlink—currently relied upon 
by Ukrainian forces—may not be as neutral or stable in the long term as hoped. There 
are several intertwined issues here. First, space is a critical frontier—not only for 
innovation and economic development but also for national and global security. 
Second, we live in a time when a company and an individual have more strategic 
capabilities than most, most countries. Third, Europe is waking up to the need for 
greater strategic autonomy, realizing it must take more responsibility for its own 
security and reduce risk in global partnerships and alliances. Fourth, Hispasat is a 
key player in Europe’s space ambitions, serving as one of the leaders of the IRIS² 
satellite constellation project, launched in December 2024. Fifth, Europe is behind in 
space technologies (and in other strategic domains) and is now trying to catch up. 
The Hispasat-Ukraine development is more than a shift in satellite providers—it’s a 
window into Europe’s evolving landscape in space, security and sovereignty. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
Space was never neutral, and it certainly isn’t today. The war in Ukraine is the first 
truly internet-powered conflict  at the battlefield   level and has introduced major 
innovations in warfare—think massive use of drones, gamification,  target acquisition 
through geolocation of mobile phones, and more. In that context, when one actor can 
control a battlefield’  s internet switch and old alliances are being tested, it may be 
time to rethink the concept of infrastructure sovereignty. Europe’s push into space is 
not just driven by Brussels or security concerns—it’s also about innovation, business 
and the future economy. German companies like Atmos Space Cargo (had the first 
flight of its Phoenix vehicle in April) and Isar Aerospace (had the first–short–test 
flight of its rocket on March 30), the Paris-based Interstellar Lab (recently announced 
----------------Page (149) Break----------------
its Petit-Prince mission to the Moon) and many others are leading the charge into the 
commercial ‘new space’ sector. Europe has no time to lose when it comes to 
energizing its slow-moving economy.

 UK Bets Big on Geoengineering 
↳ Why it matters 
The UK just allocated £56.8 million to 21 experimental climate interventions—from 
thickening Arctic ice to brightening clouds to reflect sunlight. Once considered fringe, 
geoengineering is entering the mainstream fast, with serious backing and serious 
risks. Is that good or bad? Well... it seems we will need to pursue all possible paths 
and use every tool available if we want to avoid the global disruption caused by 
human-driven climate change. If things continue as they are, the planet is on track 
for a 3-degree-plus increase by the end of the century. Climate and weather respond 
exponentially to changes in global temperature, and the differences between 1.5°C, 
2°C and 3°C scenarios are stark. The world as we know it is unlikely to exist under 
the latter—and extreme measures are increasingly likely to be needed. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
We broke the planet with technology. Now we’re hoping technology can fix it—or at 
least buy us time. The clock is ticking. Honestly, I think most people don’t care: many 
of the rich believe they can escape the chaos, and most of the population has no 
----------------Page (150) Break----------------
idea what’s coming. Remember that movie... Don’t Look Up!? Bad news aside, the 
new initiative by the UK’s Advanced Research and Invention Agency (ARIA) highlights 
two key needs: bold action to fight climate change and bold tools to drive innovation. 
ARIA’s very creation, as mentioned here before, was a response to the urgent need to 
reignite innovation and inject a moonshot mindset into British research. The real 
impact—on both fronts—remains to be seen. Great Britain is an island, and rising sea 
levels will affect it. But no man is an island, and let’s face it: we will all suffer the 
consequences of climate change.

 White Smoke, Global Signals: A New Pope for a 
Fractured World 
↳ Why does it matter? 
On May 8, the Vatican announced the election of Pope Leo XIV, formerly Cardinal 
Robert Francis Prevost of the United States—marking the first time an American has 
ascended to the papacy. This historic choice reflects the Church’s evolving global 
dynamics and the increasing influence of regions outside the traditional European 
stronghold. His background in pastoral care and administrative leadership may 
shape how the Catholic Church addresses contemporary global challenges—from 
social justice to interfaith dialogue and internal reforms—and how it positions itself 
in a world where it has been continuously losing “market share” in the global 
religious landscape. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
This election signifies   more than a change in leadership; it may signal a shift in the 
Church's engagement with the modern world. Prevost's American roots and 
experience suggest a papacy that could be more dynamic, pragmatic and focused on 
impact and engagement rather than stuck in old traditions. Let’s be clear: (1) the 
world is being torn apart by religious divisions—think of the conflict  between 
Pakistan and India, for example—and (2) Catholicism has been losing ground. It will 
be interesting to observe how Leo XIV’s leadership influences  the Church’s stance on 
pressing issues, its approach to the material needs of communities (within 
Christianity, Protestants do this much better) and its role on the international stage. 
It will also be interesting to see how digital will be factored into the future of 
Catholicism. There will be many things to follow.

 AI Regulation: To What Extent Can We Decouple 
Risk and Reward? 
↳ Why does it matter? 
In a U.S. Senate hearing this week, tech leaders like OpenAI’s Sam Altman, AMD's 
Lisa Su and Microsoft's Brad Smith supported a looser, more innovation-friendly 
approach to AI regulation. Senator Ted Cruz proposed a regulatory “sandbox” 
inspired by the freewheeling internet policies of the 1990s. The intention is to keep 
America ahead in the global AI race, especially against China. 
The contrast with Europe's regulatory approach is obvious—and so are the results 
the American economy has shown in comparison. Whoever takes the lead in the AI 
race will gain a major edge globally, set the standards and build the products other 
nations will use. But speed can be destabilizing in social, economic and geopolitical 
terms. A major risk looms: letting the genie out of the bottle before we figure out 
how to deal with truly intelligent—and possibly sentient—algorithms and machines. 
The stakes have never been this high. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
Tuning regulatory regimes to support (and not stifle)  innovation while also protecting 
citizens is a tricky but essential task. It’s not new to the world, but it has become 
more critical now. AI is moving really, really fast—we’re seeing it, right? (I'll share a 
personal experience soon)—and companies and countries are making big bets on it.  
Regulatory sandboxes have proven effective in allowing nascent technologies, 
business models and whole industries to develop and grow—from fintech in the UK 
and Brazil to agtech in Australia, from autonomous mobility in Singapore to digital 
----------------Page (153) Break----------------
health in the U.S. Multilateral banks have praised and helped disseminate this model. 
But the details of setting up a sandbox abound: economic and geographic scope? 
Timeframe? Safeguards? 
What many people don’t understand in the whole conversation about AI is the notion 
of competitiveness. Competitiveness is not absolute—it is relative by definition. 
Nations compete for talent, capital, market share and access to resources. Don’t you 
think so? Think about this: in general, if top global talent—I mean it, really top 
talent—doesn’t find capital to build a company or execute a research project in their 
home countries—A, B or C—if they don’t find other qualified   people to work with, and 
if things are hard to get done, they’re likely to move to a different country—like the 
U.S.nAnd don’t forget: water follows the path of greatest potential energy loss (that 
is, the path of least resistance). The same happens with capital and with truly top 
global talent. 
The big question from a U.S. perspective is: what is needed to build and sustain a 
leadership position against China? Institutional models and AI business strategies 
differ between the two nations. And—as Nvidia's Jensen Huang said a few days 
ago—China has 50% of all AI researchers in the world. Will changes in regulation 
alone be enough for the US to beat China? Probably not, it is a system. What will 
come out of this competition remains to be seen. 
But from a broader humanity perspective, the question is different: will we shape AI, 
or will it shape us? The answer is simple—both. The real issue is to what extent each 
of these dynamics will manifest. We are living in absolutely fascinating times, full of 
challenges and opportunities. Much more to come. 
↳ Read more here 

 News from the Climate Risk Front: NOAA Halts 
Billion-Dollar Disaster Reports 
↳ Why does it matter? 
The U.S. National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration (NOAA) has announced 
it will stop updating its widely used dataset on billion-dollar climate and weather 
disasters—a tool that has served scientists, journalists, businesses, and 
policymakers for years. This dataset has been a gold standard for tracking the 
financial toll of climate change in the U.S. The announcement came the same week 
Nature published research estimating that the share of the global population 
exposed to extreme weather will double between those born in 1960 and those born 
in 2020. Without robust data, it becomes significantly  harder to manage risk, plan 
investments, and prepare communities for growing climate-related threats. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
Climate change is real—and its impacts are being felt through severe weather that 
disrupts lives, businesses and entire communities. The need for climate data is 
urgent and global, as cities and nations must increasingly factor climate-related 
damages into their budgets and planning. While data alternatives may exist—such as 
private datasets maintained by European insurers—it remains unclear whether 
smaller municipalities and companies, particularly those outside the big corporate 
world, will have access to these tools. We must remember that cost of climate 
change doesn’t disappear when we stop tracking it. 
Globally, emerging nations—which aren’t covered in NOAA’s dataset—face even 
greater challenges. Their infrastructure is often fragile, and most are underinvesting 
due to financial constraints. Now, they must redirect resources to deal with 
escalating climate impacts. The gap in capacity between countries with 
$5,000–10,000 GDP per capita and those with $80,000 is staggering when it comes 
----------------Page (155) Break----------------
to absorbing climate-related costs. Do we have any reliable data repositories that 
offer relevant granularity for emerging (or submerging?) economies? This is a time 
when we all need more data—and we should look up (remember the movie?). 
↳ Read more here 

 Sea Bass on the moon? Aquaculture enters the

↳ Why does it matter?

simulations, laying the groundwork for potential aquaculture on the Moon. This 
----------------Page (156) Break----------------
innovation is not just a sci-fi  curiosity, it’s a step toward creating sustainable food 
sources for long-duration space missions and eventual lunar bases. Fish farming in 
space could reduce reliance on costly Earth resupplies and help close ecological 
loops in extraterrestrial habitats. 
↳ My thoughts on it

space exploration will require us not just to have food production in space but also 
manufacturing, mineral extraction, power generation, data storage, data processing 
and much more. That said, if you are interested in food and space, check out 
Interstellar Lab, a startup in which Seldor Capital invested. 

  India Suspends Indus Waters Treaty After Kashmir 
Attack 
↳ Why does it matter? 
On April 24, 2025, India suspended its participation in the Indus Waters Treaty (IWT) 
following a deadly militant attack in Pahalgam, Kashmir, which killed 26 tourists. The 
IWT, signed in 1960, has been a cornerstone of India-Pakistan relations, facilitating 
water sharing from the Indus River system. India's decision marks a significant   
escalation, as the treaty had withstood two wars between the two nuclear-armed 
countries. Pakistan has labeled the suspension an "act of war" and called for a 
neutral probe into the attack. This development threatens regional stability, as both 
nations are nuclear-armed and have a history of conflict  over Kashmir. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
While intended as a punitive measure against alleged Pakistani support for 
cross-border terrorism, the move risks undermining a long-standing mechanism that 
has managed to function amidst hostilities. The potential for miscalculation is high 
----------------Page (160) Break----------------
but, in practice, there are many doubts about whether India can truly stop the river 
from flowing into Pakistan. To compound and further complicate the situation, China 
is upstream from India on various watercourses. In any scenario, this is a clear 
display that water matters (a lot) and can be weaponized. We are likely to see more 
of this in a turbulent world under the pressure of climate change. 
↳ Read more here  
https://bit.ly/3EOWztk 
  23andMe's Bankruptcy: business and geopolitics 
get mixed 
↳ Why does it matter? 
Genetic testing company 23andMe, currently undergoing bankruptcy proceedings, 
may face national security scrutiny if it sells its assets to a foreign entity, according 
to a court filing by U.S. national security agencies. The interagency Committee on 
Foreign Investment in the United States (CFIUS) warned that any sale involving 
foreign buyers, particularly from countries like China, Russia or Iran, may be subject 
to review or prohibition if it threatens national security. The company holds genetic 
data for 15 million consumers and was a pioneer in direct-to-consumer DNA testing. 
Its failure is emblematic and, beyond the super relevant business case itself, carries 
significance  in the current global geopolitical scenario. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
23andMe was founded by Anne Wojcicki, someone at the top of the food chain in 
Silicon Valley—raised on the Stanford campus and Sergey Brin's ex-wife. This is not 
an ordinary company at all. How could it fail? Pretty standard: they struggled to 
generate recurring revenue and failed to diversify into other sources; to compound 
the situation, a data breach and regulatory friction did not help. All of that is easy to 
understand. But what really matters is history repeating itself: despite all the money, 
----------------Page (161) Break----------------
connections, talent they recruited and business acumen, they failed—it is really hard 
to see and change things when you are inside a situation. And now, geopolitics limits 
their options, a scenario that was not visible in 2013 when I was in California and 
received a 23andMe kit as a gift. Businesses now have new factors to compute 
when thinking about strategy.

  Poxiao: China's Breakthrough in Flash Memory 
Speed 
↳ Why does it matter? 
Chinese researchers at Fudan University have developed "Poxiao," a flash memory 
device capable of erasing and rewriting data in just 400 picoseconds—a picosecond 
is one trillionth of a second. This invention is approximately 100,000 times faster 
than conventional flash memory and could significantly  impact AI computing and 
data storage by reducing latency between memory and processing units. The 
technology has the potential to reshape the semiconductor industry and computers 
as we know them. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
The development of Poxiao represents a significant   leap in memory technology, 
potentially bridging the gap between storage and processing speeds. If the 
technology performs at scale, we could see two things happening. First, new 
computer architectures will emerge and the difference between RAM and storage 
units is likely to disappear—everything would become flash memory. Second, China 
could gain an edge in a critical subdomain of the semiconductor industry. However, 
challenges remain in mass production. Let's keep an eye on this and see how it 
evolves. 
----------------Page (162) Break----------------
↳ Read more here  
https://bit.ly/4lM6zUX

 The Dire Wolf Comeback: Species are blurring 
↳ Why does it matter? 
US biotech firm  Colossal Biosciences has announced the return of the dire wolf, a 
species that went extinct over 10,000 years ago. Using DNA from ancient remains 
and modern wolves as genetic scaffolds, the company has created a living, breathing 
hybrid that closely resembles the iconic predator. These new beings share 99.5% of 
their DNA with grey wolves. This breakthrough pushes the boundaries of 
de-extinction science and signals that we will increasingly see hybrid beings come to 
life. The potential for synthetic biology is tremendous, as are the ethical and 
ecological questions it raises. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
We’re entering a Jurassic Park reality — minus the T. rex (for now). Species 
conservation, ecosystem engineering and the rise of synbio are all connected in this 
story. And let’s be clear: we’re just at the beginning of the synbio evolution — expect 
a lot to happen over the next 20 years. As synbio grows and becomes more 
accessible, we’ll see a blurring of species. How different are species, genetically? 
----------------Page (167) Break----------------
Humans and chimpanzees differ by only 1–6%. The science behind the dire wolf 
“return” is fascinating — but we’ll need more than nostalgia to guide what comes 
next. 
↳ Read more here  
https://bit.ly/44kdMFo

consequences? What if things go wrong? The U.S. is placing a bet: that AI can make

 Europe’s Space Awakening 
↳ Why does it matter?

commercial space and space tech entrepreneurship seriously. Last week, Isar 
Aerospace launched a rocket from Norway—the flight lasted only 40 seconds, but the 
company gathered valuable data. With other private startups like Rocket Factory 
Augsburg (Germany) and Latitude (France) preparing for launches, Europe is pushing 
----------------Page (170) Break----------------
for independent orbital access. The EU is backing these moves with funding and 
regulatory reform, aiming to compete in the space economy, not just space science. 
It makes sense—this is a market projected to hit $1 trillion by 2040 (Morgan Stanley) 
or $1.8 trillion by 2035 (McKinsey). 
↳ My thoughts on it 
There are hopes this could be Europe’s SpaceX moment — but in plural. Rather than 
backing a single champion, Europe is fostering an ecosystem of small, focused 
rocket startups. It marks a long-overdue shift from “public prestige” programs like 
Ariane to a commercial mindset focused on speed, flexibility and iteration. If 
successful, this won’t just be about catching up — it could offer a new model for 
sovereign access to space in the private launch era. The big question: does Europe 
have the entrepreneurial drive and ability to mobilize massive resources like the US

 The brain has immense potential — remember that 
↳ Why does it matter? 
Published last week, new research using fMRI scans on awake infants reveals that 
the hippocampus—the brain's memory hub—is already active in encoding episodic 
memories by around one year of age. This challenges the long-held assumption that 
infantile amnesia stems from immature brain structures incapable of storing 
information. Instead, the findings suggest the core issue may lie in how memories 
are retrieved later in life, not in how they’re initially formed. We still know so little 
about our brains, and this opens new directions for understanding long-term memory 
consolidation and, hopefully, developing treatments to combat memory loss. As 
populations age, this will become an increasingly important frontier. 
----------------Page (174) Break----------------
↳ My thoughts on it 
If the brain encodes more than it lets us recall, memory isn’t just about storage 
capacity and integrity, but also about the paths we never learn to follow back. Can we 
learn to access those paths and unlock new potential? Can we train ourselves to 
develop new human capabilities? What technologies and protocols could enable 
that? As we uncover more about our extraordinary brains, new frontiers may emerge. 
With new discoveries, aging populations and rising mental health challenges, it’s no 
surprise the neurotechnology market is projected to reach USD 28.57 billion by 2029, 
growing at a CAGR of 13.5% since 2024. It’s something worth remembering. 
↳ Read more here  
https://bit.ly/3Rr6mZk 
https://bit.ly/41O6W9I 
 We are diving in uncharted waters 
↳ Why does it matter? 
Last Thursday, a Canadian company announced it has begun negotiations with the 
Trump administration to mine the seabed. The environmental impact could be 
massive, and environmental organizations have already protested. After more than a 
decade of work, the International Seabed Authority (ISA)—a treaty-based body with 
167 member countries (the US is not one of them)—has failed to establish global 
regulations for seabed resource exploration. In the same week, China unveiled a 
powerful new deep-sea cable cutter capable of operating at depths of up to 4,000 
meters. Developed by the China Ship Scientific   Research Centre (CSSRC), the device 
is small enough to be mounted on submersibles like the Fendouzhe. While officially 
described as a tool for civilian uses such as seabed mining or rescue missions, this 
is a textbook example of a dual-use technology. Its ability to sever undersea 
communication and power cables—critical to the global internet and energy 
----------------Page (175) Break----------------
infrastructure—raises major security concerns. The deep sea has become a hot new 
frontier, and we have no rules. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
The deep sea is a strategic frontier and one of the few environments on Earth that 
humans have not yet profoundly transformed, disrupted or degraded. Now, it’s 
becoming the stage for a modern-day Wild West rush. On the surface, we quietly 
witness the failure of our global governance system—though candidates for sheriff 
are not in short supply. Turbulent waters lie ahead. The most complex moment in our 
short history on this planet brings unprecedented challenges, yet we lack the 
systems, protocols and structures needed to confront them. Will human civilization 
stay afloat?

 China’s new silicon-free chip: A breakthrough in 
semiconductor technology? 
↳ Why does it matter? 
A research team at Peking University claims to have developed a bismuth-based 
computer chip that is 40% faster and 10% more energy-efficient  than the latest 3nm 
silicon chips from Intel and TSMC. This silicon-free approach could allow China to 
bypass restrictions imposed by U.S. sanctions and revolutionize chip design by 
eliminating some of the fundamental limitations of silicon-based semiconductors. 
With global demand for faster, more efficient chips skyrocketing, this development 
has the potential to reshape the semiconductor race and challenge the dominance of 
traditional chipmakers. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
As many analysts predict the end of Moore's Law, the race for new materials, design 
concepts, architectures, and fabrication methods for computer chips is wide open. If 
the Peking University team delivers on its promise, we could be witnessing a 
paradigm shift in semiconductors. The fact that China is leading this potential 
breakthrough adds another layer to the geopolitical chip war. But will this technology 
scale to commercial viability? And if silicon is no longer the future, which tech giants 
will adapt fastest—and who will be left behind? To close, we should not forget that 
----------------Page (182) Break----------------
competing technologies are evolving, and Moore's Law existed even before the 
transistor, as Ray Kurzweil pointed out.

 New insights into DNA repair: Could NEMO  be the 
key to overcoming chemoresistance? 
↳ Why does it matter? 
Every minute, 20 people die from cancer worldwide. The suffering and costs are 
immense. Researchers have discovered that NEMO, a protein that regulates immune 
responses, inflammation, cell survival, and proliferation, plays a crucial role in 
directing DNA repair machinery to damaged sites. NEMO helps cells repair DNA 
damage caused by UV exposure and chemotherapy. This discovery has major 
implications for cancer treatment—a high presence of NEMO correlates with 
chemoresistance and poor prognosis in colorectal cancer patients. In other words, 
NEMO repairs damaged DNA in cancer cells, but instead of restoring them to normal, 
it keeps them in a dysfunctional, cancerous state. Targeting NEMO-related pathways 
could make chemotherapy more effective in resistant tumors, save lives and lower 
costs in health systems. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
We know so little about the universe and the engines of life, but we are learning fast. 
This discovery adds another layer to our understanding of why some cancers resist 
treatment and, more importantly, hints at potential mechanisms to overcome that 
----------------Page (184) Break----------------
problem. With cancer rates rising globally, how soon can these findings translate into 
real-world therapies? The annual global economic cost of cancer represents about 
0.55% of the global GDP. The suffering? Immeasurable. This small discovery is a 
reminder of the importance of research in the field—every step matters. Moreover, it 
highlights the importance of funding research. 
.↳ Read more here  
https://bit.ly/4irTeif 
https://bit.ly/41SFZjJ

 The rise of biological AI: Can lab-grown neurons 
redefine   computing? 
↳ Why does it matter? 
Melbourne-based startup Cortical Labs launched CL1 this past week, the world’s first 
commercialized biological computer, containing lab-grown human neurons capable 
of learning. Unlike traditional AI models that require massive computational power, 
CL1 consumes just a few watts, mimicking the efficiency of biological brains. 
Researchers see potential applications in disease modeling, drug testing, and 
intelligence research, but the technology also raises ethical challenges. This was a 
‘first’, but we should expect similar products to reach the market soon. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
This breakthrough challenges the fundamental way we think about computation and 
AI—if artificial intelligence is meant to mimic the brain, why not use actual neurons? 
The potential is staggering, but so are the unknowns. How far can this technology go 
before we start questioning the ethics of creating biological intelligence? And if 
neurons can learn, how do we ensure they don’t develop traits we don’t fully 
understand? Are we on the brink of a revolution in computing, or opening a door we 
may not be ready to walk through? Questions abound, and this is just the beginning. 
Who's ready for this new world? 
↳ Read more here 

 China’s thorium discovery: A game-changer for 
global energy? 
↳ Why does it matter? China’s latest survey suggests its thorium reserves—already 
the world’s largest—may be vastly underestimated. Thorium is a mineral that holds 
two orders of magnitude more energy than uranium. If fully utilized, just one mining 
complex in China could fuel the country for 60,000 years. Thorium-based nuclear 
reactors promise a cleaner, safer alternative to fossil fuels and traditional uranium 
reactors, potentially reshaping the global energy landscape. China has been an early 
investor in thorium-based reactors. If the reserves are confirmed   and the country 
masters thorium energy, it could achieve unprecedented energy independence while 
challenging the dominance of established fuel exporters. 
↳ My thoughts on it An “endless energy source” sounds like science fiction, but if 
thorium truly delivers, it could rewrite geopolitical power dynamics. Today, uranium 
and plutonium are the primary elements used in nuclear power. Why? Because 
nuclear energy has traditionally been tied to military R&D, and both elements are 
used to make atomic bombs. Thorium, on the other hand, is not fissile and cannot be 
used to make atomic bombs, but it can serve as a fuel in nuclear reactors. A 
powerful question: with global powers racing for energy security and technology 
dominance, will thorium spark new competition—or could it help end the energy wars 
of the past century?

 A quantum chip breakthrough: hype reloaded or real 
progress? 
↳ Why does it matter? 
Microsoft claims its new chip, powered by the world’s first ‘topoconductor’ (a new 
type of material, that can create a new state of matter-not a solid, liquid, gas or 
plasma), could make practical quantum computing a reality within years rather than 
decades. If true, this would be a breakthrough on par with the invention of 
semiconductors, paving the way for ultra-powerful machines that could solve 
problems beyond classical computing’s reach—such as cracking encryption, 
revolutionizing materials science, and optimizing complex systems. However, 
physicists urge caution, noting that while the development is promising, major 
hurdles remain before quantum computing scales beyond the lab. 
----------------Page (194) Break----------------
↳ My thoughts on it 
The race to build a functional large-scale quantum computer has been full of bold 
claims, but could this be the real tipping point? DARPA has bet on it. If Microsoft’s 
technology scales up and truly enables million-qubit quantum systems, the 
implications would be staggering. Yet, quantum computing has long been a hyped 
moving target, and its biggest challenge isn’t just theoretical—it’s making these 
systems stable and commercially viable. Are we on the brink of a quantum 
revolution, or is this just another step in a marathon still far from the finish line? Have 
your qubits ready to bet! 
To close—just note that this was a project initiated by a private sector corporation 
and launched about 20 years ago. Bold! Who else in the world could undertake 
something similar? Only a very few.

 Europe needs a competitiveness shake-up 
↳ Why does it matter? 
Since 2008, GDP per capita in the Eurozone has stagnated, while the U.S. has 
experienced significant   growth. Europe faces a major competitiveness problem, 
lagging behind both the U.S. and China. It lacks the economic dynamism of these 
two global players. The war in Ukraine has further impacted the region, especially in 
terms of energy prices. European manufacturing, particularly the automotive sector, 
is struggling against Chinese competition. Meanwhile, Europe has no major tech 
companies to drive growth. The release of the European Competitiveness Compass 
last week is highly relevant in this context—Europe’s future is at stake. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
The European Union has been a success in securing peace and stability, but its 
economic dynamism is fading. While Europe offers high quality of life, it is not 
growing. Unlike the U.S., it faces demographic decline and lacks a strong innovation 
ecosystem. Germany, once a manufacturing powerhouse, is now in check as Chinese 
automakers gain market share. Rising internal tensions, exacerbated by the energy 
crisis, add to the challenge. The Competitiveness Compass released on January 29 is 
a response to Mario Draghi’s earlier report. The big question: does the EU have the 
time to implement meaningful change? With growing political opposition to Brussels

 Our great-great-grandparents may have come from 
far away 
↳ Why does it matter? 
We still don’t fully understand the origins of life on Earth. A Nature paper published 
last week provides new insights. Scientists analyzing the asteroid Bennu found that 
it contains 14 of the 20 amino acids essential for life on Earth (the building blocks of 
RAN/DNA), along with a high concentration of ammonia. This discovery strengthens 
the hypothesis that extraterrestrial materials could have contributed to life’s building 
blocks on our planet. It also sheds light on the chemical conditions during the 
formation of the solar system and the origins of organic molecules. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
We know so little about the universe—and about ourselves. Dark matter and dark 
energy make up most of the universe, while what we consider "normal" matter 
accounts for only about 5%—just a fraction. Beyond its scientific   significance,  
discoveries about the universe and the origins of life raise deep philosophical and 
existential questions about our connection to the cosmos. Practically, understanding 
the chemistry of asteroids like Bennu could help prioritize future space research and 
exploration. It may also accelerate interest in astrobiology—a field that is gaining 
----------------Page (197) Break----------------
importance. I personally know only a few astrobiologists, but not many. Do you know 
anyone working in this space? It’s a field worth watching. 
↳ Read more here 

↳ My thoughts on it

many can be users. The emergence of lower-cost, high-performance LLMs is 
changing that. We are witnessing the democratization of both access to AI and the 
ability to develop AI models. The fact that advanced reasoning models can now be 
built more efficiently means more governments and companies can become 
creators, not just users, of AI. People have recognized these new possibilities, and I 
bet more models like DeepSeek will emerge. I also expect more nations to invest in

 Rare Earths: Are We Paying Attention? 
----------------Page (202) Break----------------
↳ Why does it matter? 
The world relies heavily on rare earth materials, with demand growing at a compound 
annual growth rate (CAGR) of 9–13%. These materials are essential for batteries, 
EVs, semiconductors, electronics, and even quantum technologies. Recent 
announcements from the Chinese Academy of Sciences and news from the US and 
Africa highlight two critical trends: the search for new supply sources and the 
potential for technological breakthroughs to significantly  impact the industry. While 
rare earth materials are not truly rare, extracting them from low concentrations is 
resource-intensive—those who can lower costs may gain a competitive edge. China's 
new technology, which recovers 95% of materials in much shorter cycles while using 
60% less energy, further strengthens its dominant position. At the same time, 
investments in Africa by figures like Bill Gates and Jeff Bezos underscore the 
importance of untapped reserves and the continent’s growing global significance.  
↳ My thoughts on it 
We live in a fast-changing world, and Africa is becoming central to the global 
conversation—not just because of migration, population growth, and climate change, 
but also as a major source of natural resources. However, old models of engagement 
are unlikely to work. This is not what African countries seek, and projects that simply 
extract minerals from Africa are unlikely to be sustainable—politically, economically, 
socially, or environmentally. Meanwhile, China’s advancements in rare earth 
processing could further entrench its market dominance and serve as a powerful 
reminder that competitive advantage is not granted by nature but built by humans. 
These two trends—new resource exploration in Africa (and other parts of the world) 
and technological innovation in mineral discovery, mining, and processing—will 
significantly  shape the future of rare earths and the global economy. We should 
closely monitor how they evolve.

 How $450 is Changing the AI Game 
↳ Why does it matter? 
AI is everywhere, and its influence is expanding rapidly. However, building AI models 
is highly resource-intensive, requiring millions of GPUs, massive data centers, huge 
datasets, and significant   energy. That’s why the University of Berkeley's 
announcement of training a reasoning model for less than $450 is astonishing—not 
to mention that it is a truly "open model," with both the data used to create it and the 
algorithm to train the network disclosed. This milestone highlights how quickly AI 
costs are dropping. Just days before this announcement, NVIDIA unveiled various 
new products and technologies at CES 2025, including the scaling up of synthetic 
data use, further driving down costs and democratizing AI capabilities. 
↳ My thoughts on it

required performance level and become affordable to segments of society and the 
economy, from which they can expand to other sectors. In the AI world (our world...), 
two trends are converging: massive investments in AI infrastructure and large-scale 
projects, and rapidly declining costs to develop high-performing models—driven by 
hardware advancements, software optimization, and new conceptual models and 
architectures. Together, these trends are propelling AI to unprecedented levels of 
performance and adoption. Some believe AGI could emerge as soon as 2025 or 
2026, while AI is becoming increasingly embedded in everything and everyday life. 
Advances at the cutting edge and broad dissemination are accelerating 
simultaneously. Even with this awareness, we are likely to be surprised by what 
comes next.

 Longevity: Science Fiction or Our Next Reality? 
↳ Why does it matter? 
This week’s announcement from the Chinese Academy of Sciences, where 
researchers extended the lifespan of mice using an RNA strand derived from human 
cells, marks a promising development among many others occurring globally. 
Longevity is emerging as a major frontier for research and investment, even if the 
public is not fully aware. With a still young but aging an increasingly obese 
population, Saudi Arabia has become one of the main funders of research in the 
field. Initiatives like the $100 million XPRIZE Healthspan highlight and can accelerate 
global efforts in this field. However, living longer is not enough if it doesn’t come with 
quality of life. While lifespans have doubled over the last 100 years, many people 
spend their later years battling illnesses. This creates significant   societal and 
economic burdens—think of skyrocketing healthcare costs—while limiting society’s 
creative potential, as ill individuals cannot lead productive lives. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
As we age, our cells lose the ability to regenerate and begin producing more toxins, 
leading to inflammation  and reduced disease resistance. Restoring cellular 
regeneration is essential to achieving longevity. However, much more is still needed, 
including advancements in policy and social sciences. Some researchers believe 
that within the next 20–30 years, humanity may reach a point where we can add one 
year of life expectancy every year. The economic, social, and moral implications of 
such progress would be immense. While this might sound like science fiction, these 
breakthroughs are becoming increasingly visible—signaling a potential turning point. 
In the meantime, focus on what you can control: exercise, exercise, exercise, eat well, 
learn new things, nurture friendships and live a fulfilling life.

future may hold. As the year winds down and our gaze shifts toward what’s next, I’ve

Why does it matter?

intertwined, creating unprecedented challenges and opportunities. 
Companies, organizations of all types and nations need to think about the future to

Weekly Snapshot - December 16, 2024

 Vietnam's bet on AI & Tech 
↳ Why does it matter? 
Vietnam is one of the fastest-growing economies globally. Last week, the VinFuture 
award, a $3 million prize established by VinGroup, highlighted the country’s focus on 
technology and brought some of the world’s AI luminaries to Hanoi. NVIDIA's founder 
and CEO, Jensen Huang, Nobel Laureate Geoffrey Hinton, and Meta’s Chief AI 
Scientist Yann LeCun were among the awardees. The event showcased Vietnam's 
increasing emphasis on technology and innovation while shedding light on the 
emergence of VinGroup, a powerhouse business largely unknown in the West. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
VinGroup's story is remarkable. Founded in 1993 in Ukraine by a Vietnamese 
entrepreneur producing instant noodles, the group began operations in Vietnam in 
2000. Today, it operates across sectors such as EVs, AI, pharmaceuticals, real estate, 
fintech, healthcare, and various digital technology businesses. In some ways, it 
mirrors the rise of South Korean chaebols like Samsung. VinGroup’s founder became 
the first Vietnamese billionaire. The US-China competition has also benefited  
Vietnam in recent years, with American companies investing heavily—Apple alone 
invested at least $15 billion over five years. Vietnam's ambitions in technology are 
evident, as the country plans to train 50,000 semiconductor engineers by 2030. 
Vietnam's growth and innovation trajectory deserve more attention. 
↳ Read more here  
https://bit.ly/3P25dpO 
https://bit.ly/49I6fkI 
----------------Page (212) Break----------------
https://bit.ly/3Dcu0oA 
 From China to Europe: Rail Reshapes Trade 
↳ Why does it matter? 
Geography and logistics matter. Trans-Eurasia Logistics, a joint venture of Chinese, 
Russian, and German rail operators, now connects China to Europe by train, with a 
20-fold increase in train trips from 2015 to 2023. Sending a container by rail from 
China to Germany or Poland now costs half as much as by sea. Global conflicts  are 
reshaping logistics—for instance, shipping between China and Europe via the Suez 
Canal has declined by 67 percent. The New Silk Road rail line not only transforms 
global trade routes but also significantly  impacts the economies of Central Asia, 
through which it passes. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
Central Asia is undergoing rapid change. During a recent call with a colleague there, 
the head of a major global consultancy reported tripling its business and team in 
Uzbekistan in less than six months. The conflict  in Ukraine has redirected 
investments to Central Asia, while the conflict  in the Middle East has increased the 
costs and risks of sea transportation in the Red Sea, benefiting  the New Silk Road. 
Together, these factors are accelerating growth and transformation in the region. 
This is also part of a larger shift in global trade dynamics. China now accounts for 
15% of Europe’s foreign trade, closing in on the U.S.'s 17%. These developments 
should be closely followed by those looking for new global business opportunities. 
↳ Read more here  
https://bit.ly/4fkaqnK 
https://bit.ly/3BxcmLS 
----------------Page (213) Break----------------
 Graphene Chips: The Future Beyond Moore's Law 
↳ Why does it matter? 
Our world depends on computation and semiconductors. However, we are now 
reaching the physical limits of current semiconductor technology, making it 
increasingly difficult  to keep up with Moore's Law—the idea, coined by Intel 
co-founder Gordon Moore, that the performance of computer chips doubles every 18 
months. With 3 nanometers as the node size in chips, we are approaching the 
physical theoretical minimum. A recently announced development from 
California-based Destination 2D could open a new frontier by solving key challenges 
in manufacturing chips with graphene. This breakthrough could extend Moore's Law 
and revolutionize electronics. 
↳ My thoughts on it 
As Ray Kurzweil has demonstrated, Moore's Law has held since the early 20th 
century, even before it was stated by Gordon Moore. However, the limits of 
semiconductor physics are clear. While quantum computing holds promise, it is not 
ready yet. Graphene, discovered decades ago and awarded the 2010 Nobel Prize, 
might finally be ready for practical applications. As with all new technologies, early 
performance often falls short, but this could be the turning point. Destination 2D’s 
new technology, using pressure deposition methods, could unlock graphene's 
potential for microelectronics. Has the time come for graphene chips? Make your 
bets. 
↳ Read more here  
https://bit.ly/4gzOeqO 
https://bit.ly/3DjoYGX 
https://bit.ly/3ZC9x4k

 An iron breakthrough 
↳ Why does it matter? 
Steel is fundamental to our lives and all the things we manufacture—if not made of 
steel, made with steel. Recent developments in China regarding a new, rapid process 
for producing iron (and thus steel) hold significant   promise and could further 
consolidate China's leadership; today, the country produces 54% of the world's crude 
steel. Steelmaking and ironmaking are major carbon emitters. The new flash 
smelting technology can be transformational, as it does not use coal and can be 
used to decarbonize the industry. The process also performs with low-grade iron 
ores, and its future adoption could allow for the substitution of iron ore from Brazil 
and Australia with minerals mined in China. 
----------------Page (215) Break----------------
↳ My thoughts on it 
China's dominance in steel production is staggering, producing seven times more 
than the second-largest producer, India. To be fair, the country's industrial prowess in 
iron and steelmaking dates back centuries, reaching in the 11th century a volume of 
production that Europe would only achieve in the 18th century. It's remarkable to see 
a centuries-old technology being reimagined. While scaling this new process 
economically may take time, it represents a significant   step for decarbonization and 
China's competitive edge. This development is worth following closely, as it could 
impact every manufacturing sector globally. 
↳ Read more here  
https://bit.ly/3BpsRtl 
https://bit.ly/4f9j4VZ 
https://bit.ly/4f8ROqO 
 Quantum is getting physical 
↳ Why does it matter? 
Quantum computing is a major frontier, promising to process information far faster 
than today's computers. However, two key challenges remain: eliminating errors and 
achieving scalability. Quantum computers are sensitive, often producing errors, and 
current models operate with only a few qubits. A recent development from 
Switzerland is groundbreaking because it introduces physical qubits not based on 
electrons, realizing a concept that has been around for decades. This advancement 
can open new frontiers in computation. 
----------------Page (216) Break----------------
↳ My thoughts on it 
Computing power has followed Moore's law since the early 20th century, even before 
semiconductors. As we near the physical limits of microchips, quantum computing 
offers incredible potential. Like other transformative technologies, quantum is in its 
early stages and the "deception phase." Progress will require testing various 
solutions and architectures to create scalable, functional quantum computers. This 
new approach demonstrates the potential to innovate beyond traditional 
electron-based computing. It's a clear reminder that quantum isn't just about 
improvement—it's about reimagining computing altogether. 
↳ Read more here  
https://bit.ly/3ORNGAA 
https://bit.ly/3ZKTZfP 
https://bit.ly/3Bbpjeh 
 Pipelines matter 
↳ Why does it matter? 
Meta's plan to build a 40,000 km subsea fiber-optic cable underscores the critical 
role of infrastructure in the digital and AI era. Control over physical assets like data 
centers and the cables connecting them is increasingly important. Data centers are 
the fastest-growing energy consumption category globally. Companies like Meta, 
Microsoft, and Google are not just creating algorithms but also building and 
powering their own infrastructure to ensure better service and reduce risks, including 
dependence on telecommunications companies. 
----------------Page (217) Break----------------
↳ My thoughts on it 
By 2030, U.S. data centers are projected to consume 8–10% of all electricity, up from 
3.5% in 2023. Globally, data center energy consumption is expected to grow by nearly 
20% annually through 2028. Meta alone handles 10% of the world’s data traffic  and 
22% of all mobile traffic.  Owning infrastructure ensures quality, minimizes risk, and 
reduces reliance on telecom companies. Countries with clean energy resources have 
a significant   opportunity to attract investment by building sustainable, low-carbon 
data centers, creating a competitive edge in the digital economy. 
↳ Read more here  
https://bit.ly/4fd6yEW 
https://bit.ly/4f9Xi4v
